# Volume 3 — Soul, R&B, Funk

*Top 10 Albums of All Time, by Sub-Genre*

---

## On the Volume

Volume 1 laid out four root traditions — Blues, Folk, Country, Gospel — and treated them as the river system from which essentially everything in twentieth-century American popular music flows. Volume 2 took the first major downstream tradition, Jazz, and spent seventeen sub-genre files inside it. Volume 3 takes the second major downstream tradition: the body of Black American secular popular music that runs alongside jazz from the 1940s forward, splits off from blues and gospel into the commercial recording industry, and over the next sixty years generates the genres that this volume covers — Soul, R&B, and Funk, plus the disco-and-dance branch that extends them out onto the floor. Twenty-two sub-genres in the current draft, organized into three parts, debated through the same Scaruffi-versus-Fantano process the first two volumes established and adjudicated through the same coin-flip apparatus when the argument cannot move either critic.

The volume sits structurally next to Volume 2. The two traditions share personnel, share infrastructure, share rhythmic and harmonic vocabularies, and at multiple points share records. Soul jazz is in Volume 2 because the records were rated by jazz critics and made for jazz-club audiences; jazz-funk is in this volume because the records were rated by funk and R&B audiences and made for a different listening culture, even when the musicians were the same. The boundary between the two volumes is occasionally arbitrary in the way the boundary between two adjacent and overlapping fields always is. The cross-listings between Volume 2 and Volume 3 run dense in both directions, and the reader who works through both volumes will find the same Booker T. and the MGs sidemen, the same Memphis horn sections, the same Donald Byrd and Roy Ayers sessions, and the same hip-hop sampling apparatus reaching back into both canons.

## The Unifying Thesis

The argument that organizes the volume is that these three traditions — R&B and soul, funk, disco and dance — are best read as three successive phases of a single project: the development of Black American popular music as a music of dance and emotion in which the *primary* artistic locus shifts decade by decade through the elements of the band.

Phase one is singing. The R&B and soul lineage that runs from the late-1940s urban-blues vocal groups and the doo-wop street-corner harmonizers through Motown's pop-soul factory, Stax's grittier southern soul, Philadelphia's lush orchestral pop, the psychedelic-soul laboratory at the end of the 1960s, the quiet-storm late-night format, the neo-soul revival of the mid-1990s, contemporary R&B's chart presence, new jack swing's hip-hop fusion, and PBR&B's atmospheric reinvention — is a tradition in which the *singer* is the central artistic object. The arrangements get more sophisticated, the production gets denser, the instrumental vocabulary changes radically; but the form's center of gravity stays on the human voice, on what a vocalist can do with melody and phrasing and emotional disclosure inside a verse-chorus structure. The R&B and soul section of this volume covers that arc.

Phase two is rhythm. By the mid-1960s a wing of the soul tradition — anchored in James Brown's increasingly rhythm-first arrangements from "Out of Sight" (1964) and "Papa's Got a Brand New Bag" (1965) forward — had begun reorganizing the music around the *band* rather than the singer, treating the rhythm section as the primary artistic agent and the vocal as one element of a larger groove architecture. The funk that emerged from that reorganization, and the parallel funk traditions that consolidated through the 1970s — Parliament-Funkadelic's cosmic theatre, the Washington-DC go-go continuum, the early-1980s boogie wing of synthesizer-and-drum-machine funk, the funk-rock collision with electric guitar, the jazz-funk crossover that this volume cross-references with Volume 2, and the Minneapolis Sound's Prince-led pop-funk synthesis — is a tradition in which the singer is still important but the rhythm section has become the primary composer. The funk section of this volume covers that arc.

Phase three is dance. By the mid-1970s a wing of the soul-and-funk tradition had moved into the disco-club format — Black, Latino, and gay-coded New York and Philadelphia and West Coast clubs whose central artistic act was a DJ extending and remixing records for dancers, and whose central audience identity was constituted around movement rather than around listening. The disco that emerged from that move, and the dance traditions that descended from it — Hi-NRG in San Francisco and the UK, Eurodisco and Italo in Munich and Milan, the post-disco fragmentation in early-1980s New York that fed into boogie, freestyle, and the first Chicago house records — is a tradition in which dance is no longer the assumed use of the record but the *explicit subject* of the record. The disco-and-dance section of this volume covers that arc.

All three phases feed back into each other. Funk re-enters R&B through the late-1970s and 1980s charts (Earth Wind and Fire, the Commodores, Rick James, Prince crossing both ways); disco re-enters R&B through the late-1970s soul charts (Donna Summer, Diana Ross's *Diana*, the entire post-disco boogie wing); and all three feed forward into hip-hop, which inherits the funk break, the R&B vocal, and the disco-12-inch-DJ format and recombines them into the dominant Black popular music of the post-1979 era. The volume tries to keep those feedback loops visible. The cross-listings between the three parts run dense, and the reading order is recommended but not required.

## Methodology Recap, with a Volume-Specific Caveat

The debate process is the same as Volumes 1 and 2: independent top-15s from each critic, consensus placements where they overlap, argument over the contested slots, coin flips when the argument cannot move either critic. Coin-flip resolutions are recorded explicitly in each file. Honorable mentions follow. Cross-listings are flagged, and the cross-listings into Volume 2 (soul jazz, jazz-funk, fusion) are flagged as such rather than treated as internal references.

The volume-specific caveat about the Scaruffi/Fantano matchup is the inverse of Volume 2's. In the jazz volume the coverage asymmetry ran heavily toward Scaruffi — he had a comprehensive jazz canon, Fantano's coverage thinned out as the tradition moved backward in time. In this volume the asymmetry is gentler and runs in both directions depending on which sub-genre is in question. **Fantano's strongest territory in this volume is the contemporary end — neo-soul forward.** He has direct, granular, repeatedly-stated critical positions on D'Angelo, Erykah Badu, Lauryn Hill, Bilal, Maxwell, Jill Scott, Common, Frank Ocean, Solange, SZA, FKA twigs, Kelela, Blood Orange, the Weeknd, Anderson .Paak, Steve Lacy, Tyler the Creator's *Igor* and *Flower Boy*, and the broader PBR&B and contemporary-R&B catalog through the present. He has reviewed almost every major record in those sub-genres at length, and the placements there are arrived at through direct critical work rather than extrapolation. The neo-soul, contemporary R&B, and PBR&B files in particular are the most Fantano-direct files in any of the three volumes so far.

His coverage thins as the tradition moves backward. The mid-period sub-genres — Philadelphia soul, psychedelic soul, quiet storm, classic funk, P-Funk, jazz-funk, the Minneapolis Sound — are covered through occasional retrospective reviews and through what can be reconstructed from his stated criteria, but the placements are mostly Scaruffi-anchored with Fantano positions extrapolated and flagged. The earliest sub-genres — doo-wop, early R&B, Motown, Stax/Southern soul — have essentially no direct Fantano coverage and are constructed almost entirely from Scaruffi and from the editor's reading of what Fantano's documented preferences would produce. The disco-and-dance part is in the middle: Scaruffi has long-form positions on the disco canon and on the post-disco / Arthur Russell axis, Fantano has direct reviews of certain contemporary dance-pop and disco-revival records but not of the canonical 1977-1982 disco material. The flagging convention from Volumes 1 and 2 carries over and gets a heavy workout in the earlier sub-genres.

The other structural fact worth naming at the front of the volume is that Scaruffi's voice is *less* dominant here than it was in Volume 2. He has a strong canon on soul (he treats the Stax / Hi / Atlantic axis and the psychedelic-soul wing as artistic peaks of the 1960s) and on funk (Sly Stone, James Brown, P-Funk, Prince all rated very high in his system), but his disco coverage is more reserved than his jazz coverage, his contemporary-R&B coverage thins out past roughly 2010, and his Motown ratings are systematically lower than the consensus places them — Scaruffi reads Motown as a pop-craft achievement rather than as an artistic peak, and the Motown file argues through that disagreement explicitly. The volume is less Scaruffi-weighted than Volume 2 was, and the contemporary files in particular have the quality of working-critic agreement between two critics with overlapping but distinct positions, which is closer to what the methodology was designed to produce.

## Reading Instructions

The volume is organized in three parts that track the three-phase argument above.

**Part One — R&B and Soul** covers the eleven sub-genres in the singing-first lineage: doo-wop, early R&B, Motown, Stax / Southern soul, Philadelphia soul, psychedelic soul, quiet storm, neo-soul, contemporary R&B, new jack swing, and PBR&B. The arc runs from the late-1940s street-corner harmonies through the present-day Frank Ocean / Solange / SZA atmospheric wing. Eleven sub-genres.

**Part Two — Funk** covers the seven sub-genres in the rhythm-first lineage: classic funk, P-Funk, go-go, boogie / electro-funk, funk-rock, jazz-funk, and the Minneapolis Sound. The arc runs from James Brown's mid-1960s rhythm-section reorganization through the Prince-led 1980s synthesis. Seven sub-genres.

**Part Three — Disco and Dance** covers the four sub-genres in the dance-as-subject lineage: disco, Hi-NRG, Eurodisco, and post-disco. The arc runs from disco's 1977-1979 commercial apex through the early-1980s post-disco fragmentation that fed into house, freestyle, and the broader dance-music apparatus that gets its own treatment in a later volume. Four sub-genres.

Each part has its own short introduction. Each sub-genre file is self-contained and can be read on its own. The recommended reading order is the order the volume is bound in, because the dependencies run forward — funk reads against the soul that preceded it, disco reads against the funk and soul that fed it, PBR&B reads against everything that came before — but reading any individual file in isolation also works.

A standing scope note. The album-as-unit-of-analysis fits this volume's traditions unevenly, in the same way it fit Volume 2 unevenly. Doo-wop is a singles culture from end to end (the doo-wop file works compilations as anchors and is honest about it). Early R&B is also heavily compilation-based. Motown's commercial peak is in the singles charts, though the Motown LP catalog from roughly 1971 forward (Marvin Gaye's *What's Going On*, Stevie Wonder's classic-period albums, the late Diana Ross sides) is genuine album-form material. The funk canon, the disco canon, and the post-neo-soul R&B canon are all properly album-form. The convention is the same as the prior volumes: where compilations are the canonical artifact, the lists treat them as such and flag the convention; where albums are the canonical artifact, the lists treat them as such. This is standard practice in the criticism, and both critics' published work treats the conventions the same way.

## Part One — R&B and Soul

Eleven sub-genres, roughly seventy-five years. The arc this part covers runs from the late-1940s urban-blues vocal groups and the street-corner doo-wop harmonizers of the early 1950s through to the present-day PBR&B wing represented by Frank Ocean, Solange, SZA, and the contemporary atmospheric-R&B catalog. Inside that arc the form does almost everything popular music can do at the level of human-voice-centered Black American song: it invents the post-blues vocal-group format in the doo-wop and early-R&B singles era; it builds the small-band horn-driven R&B vocabulary that the rock-and-roll boom of the mid-1950s will partially commercialize; it generates the two great mid-1960s soul axes, Motown's Detroit-based pop-soul factory and Stax's Memphis-based gospel-blooded southern soul, which constitute the form's first artistic peak and its first sustained chart presence; it builds the Philadelphia-International-Records orchestral-soul refinement of that vocabulary in the late 1960s and early 1970s; it generates the psychedelic-soul laboratory at the end of the 1960s, in which Sly Stone, Norman Whitfield's productions for the Temptations, and the late Marvin Gaye work absorb rock studio-craft into the soul tradition; it consolidates the late-night-radio quiet-storm format around Smokey Robinson's eponymous 1975 record and the broader Marvin Gaye / Anita Baker / Sade lineage; it generates the neo-soul revival of the mid-1990s out of Soulquarians collective work; it produces the contemporary-R&B chart presence that runs from Mariah Carey and Boyz II Men through Beyoncé and into the present; it builds the new-jack-swing hip-hop-fusion bridge with Teddy Riley's productions in the late 1980s; and it generates the PBR&B atmospheric reinvention of the form from roughly 2009 forward.

**Doo-Wop** is the foundation, in the same structural sense that Dixieland is the foundation for Volume 2. Between roughly 1953 and 1962, thousands of vocal groups — most of them Black, many of them teenaged, working out of Harlem and Brooklyn and Philadelphia and Detroit and Chicago — cut 45s for small independent labels (Red Robin, End, Gee, Rama, Herald, Chess, Vee-Jay, the early Atlantic catalog) that survived in jukeboxes and on regional radio. The form is a singles culture from end to end; almost no doo-wop group made a coherent album, and the canon is therefore a compilation canon. The file works the compilation problem honestly. The Italian-American street-corner groups that integrated the form in the late 1950s (the Belmonts, the Skyliners, the Dell-Vikings) get treated as the integrationist edge rather than as the form's center.

**Early R&B** is the broader instrumental-and-vocal field around doo-wop. The Atlantic catalog's late-1940s and 1950s output (Ruth Brown, LaVern Baker, Big Joe Turner, Ray Charles's first Atlantic sides), the Specialty catalog (Little Richard's New Orleans sessions with Bumps Blackwell), the Imperial Records New Orleans work (Fats Domino with Dave Bartholomew producing), the Chess R&B catalog beyond the blues sides, the King Records output (Wynonie Harris, Roy Brown's "Good Rocking Tonight" lineage, Hank Ballard and the Midnighters), and the early Sun Records R&B catalog before it pivoted to rockabilly all live in this file. The form bleeds into the rock-and-roll origins covered in Volume 4 — most of what gets called "early rock and roll" is structurally early R&B with white singers covering Black material — and the cross-listings into Volume 1's pre-war blues and into Volume 4 are dense.

**Motown** is the pop-soul factory. From its founding by Berry Gordy in Detroit in 1959 through its move to Los Angeles in 1972 and into the late-1970s catalog, Motown Records (and its subsidiary labels Tamla, Gordy, Soul, V.I.P., Rare Earth) produced the most commercially successful catalog of Black popular music the recording industry had yet generated. The Holland-Dozier-Holland songwriting and production team, the Funk Brothers as the house rhythm section, the Smokey Robinson lineage as the in-house songwriter-and-singer, the Supremes / Temptations / Four Tops / Marvelettes / Martha and the Vandellas / Stevie Wonder / Marvin Gaye / Jackson 5 / Gladys Knight roster — the file works through the body of work that constitutes the form's first sustained crossover into the white-pop chart mainstream. This is the file where Scaruffi's lower rating diverges most sharply from consensus; the editor argues with him in the file.

**Stax / Southern Soul** is the Memphis-based parallel. From 1961 (Stax Records' founding by Jim Stewart and Estelle Axton on McLemore Avenue in Memphis) through the early 1970s, the southern-soul wing — Stax in Memphis, Hi Records also in Memphis, Muscle Shoals Sound Studio in Alabama, the Atlantic-distributed Aretha Franklin sessions cut partly at Muscle Shoals and partly in New York, the smaller Goldwax and Volt and Spar labels — produced the form's grittier, gospel-blooded, more rhythm-section-driven counterweight to Motown's polished pop-soul. Booker T. and the MGs as the Stax house band, the Memphis Horns, Otis Redding, Sam and Dave, Wilson Pickett, Eddie Floyd, Carla Thomas, Rufus Thomas, Isaac Hayes, the Staple Singers — and the parallel Hi Records roster of Al Green, Ann Peebles, O. V. Wright, and Syl Johnson, with Willie Mitchell producing — constitute the file's body. Scaruffi prefers Stax to Motown by a wide margin; Fantano (extrapolated) is more agnostic but recognizes the Stax catalog's deeper sonic distinctiveness.

**Philadelphia Soul** is the orchestral refinement. From roughly 1971 (Philadelphia International Records' founding by Kenny Gamble and Leon Huff, with Thom Bell as the third major producer-arranger of the axis) through the late 1970s, the Philadelphia sound — lush string arrangements, prominent vibes and harp, Earl Young's hi-hat-driven drumming as a direct precursor to the disco backbeat, MFSB as the house rhythm section, the O'Jays / Harold Melvin and the Blue Notes / Spinners / Stylistics / Delfonics roster — produced the most sophisticated pop-soul of the early 1970s. The file works the Philadelphia / disco boundary explicitly; the late Philadelphia International catalog feeds directly into the disco file in Part Three.

**Psychedelic Soul** is the laboratory. From roughly 1968 (Sly and the Family Stone's *Dance to the Music* and *Stand!*, Norman Whitfield's first productions for the Temptations with "Cloud Nine") through the early 1970s, a wing of the soul tradition absorbs rock-era studio craft, extended-form song structures, social-and-political subject matter, and a more adventurous harmonic and timbral vocabulary. Sly Stone, the Whitfield-produced Temptations (*Cloud Nine*, *Psychedelic Shack*, *All Directions*, *Masterpiece*), the late Marvin Gaye work (*What's Going On*, *Let's Get It On*, *I Want You*), Stevie Wonder's classic period from *Music of My Mind* (1972) through *Songs in the Key of Life* (1976) — five albums that constitute the deepest single-artist run in the volume — and the broader experimental-soul catalog (the Undisputed Truth, Funkadelic when it edges into soul, the Isley Brothers' T-Neck records). This is one of the files where Scaruffi's coverage is strongest and where his rankings carry most weight.

**Quiet Storm** is the late-night-radio format. The form's name comes from Smokey Robinson's 1975 album *A Quiet Storm* and from the subsequent late-night-format radio shows that the album's atmospheric mid-tempo soul template inspired. The file works the format-as-genre question honestly — quiet storm is partly a programming category and partly a coherent musical sub-genre — and treats the Marvin Gaye late catalog, the Anita Baker / Sade / Whitney Houston axis of the 1980s, and the broader adult-contemporary R&B wing as the form's body. The cross-listings into contemporary R&B are dense.

**Neo-Soul** is the revival. From roughly 1995 (D'Angelo's *Brown Sugar*) through the early 2000s, the Soulquarians collective (D'Angelo, Erykah Badu, Common, the Roots, J Dilla, Bilal, Q-Tip in his post-Tribe orbit, Mos Def, Talib Kweli) and the broader neo-soul wave (Lauryn Hill's *The Miseducation*, Jill Scott, Maxwell, Angie Stone, Macy Gray, India.Arie, Musiq Soulchild) revived the live-band-and-real-instruments soul aesthetic against the dominant late-1990s chart sound. This is the first file in the volume where Fantano's direct critical work is dense — he has covered the canonical neo-soul records repeatedly and his positions on D'Angelo's *Voodoo* (2000) in particular are unusually well-developed.

**Contemporary R&B** is the chart presence. From roughly the late 1980s forward, the mainstream R&B chart and its associated production apparatus (Babyface and L.A. Reid, Jam and Lewis, Teddy Riley before he becomes the new jack swing figure, Rodney Jerkins, Timbaland and Missy Elliott, the Neptunes, Bryan-Michael Cox, Stargate, more recent figures like No I.D., Mike WiLL Made-It) generated the form's chart-pop continuation. Mariah Carey, Boyz II Men, TLC, Aaliyah, Brandy, Monica, Usher, Mary J. Blige, Beyoncé as a Destiny's Child member and then as a solo artist, R. Kelly with the appropriate ethical flagging, Alicia Keys, John Legend — the file works the body of work that constitutes the chart-R&B mainstream of the 1990s, 2000s, and 2010s.

**New Jack Swing** is the hip-hop fusion. From roughly 1987 (Teddy Riley's productions for Keith Sweat, Bobby Brown, and Guy) through the early 1990s, a tight cluster of producers (Riley primarily, Babyface and L.A. Reid secondarily, Jimmy Jam and Terry Lewis in their pop-funk mode) fused hip-hop drum programming and 808 bass with R&B vocal arrangements and produced the form that bridged the soul tradition into the hip-hop production era. Guy, Bobby Brown's *Don't Be Cruel*, Keith Sweat, Bell Biv DeVoe, Jodeci's early records, the Boyz II Men *Cooleyhighharmony* sessions, the early Mary J. Blige work under Sean "Puffy" Combs's direction at Uptown — the file works through the form's tight commercial window before the wave broke into the broader contemporary R&B mainstream.

**PBR&B** is the atmospheric revolution. From roughly 2009 (the Weeknd's *House of Balloons* mixtape, How to Dress Well's *Love Remains*, the early James Blake material) through the present, a wing of contemporary R&B has reorganized the form around atmospheric production, slower tempos, more harmonically adventurous chord vocabulary, hip-hop drum patterns played at half-time or slower, and a more introspective vocal register. Frank Ocean's *Channel Orange* (2012) and *Blonde* (2016), Solange's *A Seat at the Table* (2016) and *When I Get Home* (2019), SZA's *CTRL* (2017), FKA twigs's *LP1* (2014) and *Magdalene* (2019), Kelela's *Take Me Apart* (2017), Blood Orange's catalog, the Weeknd's full trajectory from mixtapes through *After Hours* (2020), Steve Lacy, Daniel Caesar, dvsn, Jorja Smith, Snoh Aalegra, Cleo Sol — the file is one of the most contemporary-direct in any of the three volumes, with Fantano's documented reviews covering essentially every canonical entry.

Eleven sub-genres. The arc they describe is the singing-first phase of the volume's overall three-phase argument. The reading instruction for this part is straightforward: read the files in the order they appear, because the dependencies run forward and because each successive sub-genre is in dialogue with the ones that preceded it. Motown reads against doo-wop and early R&B; Stax reads against Motown; psychedelic soul reads against the mid-1960s Stax / Motown mainstream; neo-soul reads against the late-1980s and 1990s contemporary R&B chart; PBR&B reads against everything that came before. The cross-listings into Part Two and Part Three of the volume are flagged where they appear.

---

### Doo-Wop
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

Doo-wop is the genre that most violently resists the album-format ranking exercise. It is a singles culture from end to end. Between roughly 1953 and 1962, thousands of vocal groups — most of them Black, many of them teenaged, working out of Harlem stairwells, Brooklyn schoolyards, and Philadelphia rec rooms — cut 45s for tiny labels (Red Robin, Old Town, End, Gee, Rama, Herald) that survived in jukeboxes and on regional radio. Almost none of these groups made a coherent LP. The handful of LPs that did appear in period were typically singles-plus-filler issued by the label after a chart hit, and most of those are out of print, unavailable digitally, or have been superseded by compilations.

So the canon is a compilation canon, and any honest top-10 has to admit that openly. Both critics had to be reconstructed from their actual behavior toward the form. Scaruffi treats doo-wop with respectful distance — he files it under the early-rock-and-roll lineage in his *History of Rock Music*, gives credit to the form's haunting outliers (he writes warmly of The Flamingos, The Moonglows, The Five Satins, Frankie Lymon), but is cooler on the slick uptown groups that crossed to white teen radio (The Platters, the post-McPhatter Drifters). His preferences track toward the eerie minor-key ballad and away from the white pop-crossover novelty. Fantano has produced essentially zero direct doo-wop commentary across 15+ years of *theneedledrop* — the form predates his coverage window by three decades and lives on radio formats he has never engaged with. All Fantano positions in this essay are EXTRAPOLATED from his documented preferences: he favors songwriting craft, vocal personality, production weirdness, and artist-as-author framings; he distrusts pure nostalgia objects and grandparent-canon consensus picks; he gravitates to the rawer, the stranger, and the Black-originated over the white-covered. These extrapolations are flagged throughout.

The debate ran six rounds. Round one was Top-15 lists; round two was the compilation-vs-album problem (resolved: compilations count, this is doo-wop, the alternative is no list); round three argued the slick-vs-haunting axis (Scaruffi pulls toward the Flamingos/Moonglows pole, extrapolated-Fantano pulls toward the rougher Black groups and away from the Italian-American revivalists); round four argued white doo-wop's place (the Belmonts, the Skyliners, the Dell-Vikings as the integrationist edge — both critics skeptical for different reasons); round five was the Frankie Lymon question (was a 13-year-old kid the form's greatest vocalist, or a novelty?); round six was sequencing and the coin-flips. Three irreconcilables went to coin-flip.

A note on the box sets cited: Rhino's *The Doo Wop Box* (1993, four CDs, 101 tracks) and its sequel *The Doo Wop Box II* (1996) are the spine of the canon. Atlantic's *Atlantic Rhythm and Blues 1947-1974* (eight-volume box, Atlantic/Rhino) catches the Drifters/Clovers/Coasters Atlantic years. Chess assembled various doo-wop and vocal-group anthologies under the *Chess Vocal Groups* and *The Best of Chess Doo Wop* banners (sources vary on exact titles — cited cautiously). Bear Family has issued group-specific multi-disc retrospectives for several major acts (the most reliable Bear Family doo-wop sets I am confident exist are their Coasters and Platters retrospectives — I am NOT inventing specific titles I cannot verify). Ace Records UK has been the most active doo-wop reissue label of the last twenty years and runs reliable single-label series. Where I cite a compilation below, I am citing a release I am confident exists; where I'm uncertain, I say so.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Various Artists — *The Doo Wop Box* (Rhino, 1993)

If doo-wop is a compilation genre, then the compilation that defines the genre is the album of the genre. Rhino's four-CD, 101-track box, assembled by Bob Hyde and the Rhino reissue team in the early '90s and packaged with a thick essay book, is the consensus canonical doo-wop document. It sequences from the Ravens and Orioles of the late '40s through the Italian-American street groups of the early '60s, covering majors and minors, Black originators and white revivalists, slick uptown and raw street-corner. The "Earth Angel" / "In the Still of the Nite" / "Why Do Fools Fall in Love" / "Sh-Boom" / "Goodnight Sweetheart, Goodnight" / "Gloria" / "I Only Have Eyes for You" / "Speedo" / "Get a Job" arc is here, in something close to chronological order, with the haunting B-side material from the Flamingos and Moonglows that radio cherry-picking would have buried. Scaruffi accepts this as the form's center of gravity even though he'd weight the haunting material higher than Rhino does. Fantano (extrapolated) is skeptical of compilation-as-art objects in principle, but the box's sequencing has a curatorial argument — it makes the case that doo-wop is a Black vocal tradition that white groups joined late and successfully — and that argument is the kind of structural claim he respects. Both agree it has to be number one because there is no album-form alternative that covers the field. The fact that this is a compilation rather than a record by an artist is the entire honest argument about doo-wop.

##### 2. The Flamingos — *Flamingo Serenade* (End, 1959)

The Flamingos are Scaruffi's favorite doo-wop group, and *Flamingo Serenade* is one of the few actual LPs of the era that holds together as an album rather than a singles-plus-filler product. Built around their immortal cover of Harry Warren and Al Dubin's "I Only Have Eyes for You" — the Terry Johnson arrangement with the "doo-bop sh-bop" floating-vowel backing vocal, recorded for End Records in 1959 with the original Chicago-to-New York lineup of Nate Nelson on lead, Zeke Carey, Jake Carey, Paul Wilson, Tommy Hunt, and Terry Johnson — the record reframes Tin Pan Alley standards through a vocal-group lens that is closer to chamber music than to street-corner harmony. "Time Was," "But Not for Me," "Yours," "Begin the Beguine," "I'm in the Mood for Love" — the song selection is conservative; the arrangements are not. Nelson's lead is restrained and slightly haunted, and the backing vocals are mixed up to near-instrumental status. Scaruffi cites the Flamingos as evidence that doo-wop could reach for art-song atmosphere; this is the album that makes that case most clearly. Fantano (extrapolated) would be drawn to the Terry Johnson arrangements — they are genuinely strange productions for 1959, with reverb depth and vocal layering that anticipates the Wall of Sound by two years. Both critics push this to number two ahead of higher-charting groups because it is a real album by a real group that survives as art and not as nostalgia. The End Records LP is reissued on Collectables and on various CD packages; the most complete Flamingos retrospective is on Rhino's *The Doo Wop Box* and on dedicated Flamingos anthologies.

##### 3. The Coasters — *The Coasters' Greatest Hits* (Atco, 1959) / Atlantic R&B Box (Atlantic/Rhino)

The Coasters are technically a vocal group but functionally a Leiber-and-Stoller vehicle, and that authorial dimension is what makes them survive the singles-era problem in album form. From 1957 to 1959, working with the writer-producers Jerry Leiber and Mike Stoller out of Atlantic/Atco's New York operation, the Coasters (Carl Gardner, Billy Guy, Cornell Gunter, Will "Dub" Jones, with King Curtis on tenor sax) cut "Searchin'," "Young Blood," "Yakety Yak," "Charlie Brown," "Along Came Jones," "Poison Ivy," and "Shoppin' for Clothes" — a run of comic teen-life vignettes that doubles as proto-rap narrative structure and as some of the sharpest songwriting of the rock-and-roll era. The 1959 Atco *Greatest Hits* LP is the canonical period document; the more complete view is on the Atlantic R&B box (*Atlantic Rhythm and Blues 1947-1974*) and on Rhino's dedicated Coasters compilations. Scaruffi rates the Coasters high because the Leiber-Stoller songs are character-driven miniature scripts and because King Curtis's saxophone work is musically substantial. Fantano (extrapolated) would over-rate the Coasters relative to consensus — the songwriting craft and the implicit racial and class commentary in "Shoppin' for Clothes" are exactly the kind of doubled-meaning surface that he tends to elevate, and the comic-narrative mode prefigures hip-hop storytelling in ways a listener born in 1985 would notice. Both critics agree the Coasters are the only doo-wop-adjacent group whose body of work coheres as authored albums-worth of material rather than as a hits-and-filler compilation.

##### 4. The Drifters — *Rockin' & Driftin'* (Atlantic, 1958) / Clyde McPhatter–Era Compilations

The Drifters problem: there are two Drifters. The original McPhatter-led group (1953-1955, with Clyde McPhatter on lead before he left for solo work and the Army) cut "Money Honey," "Such a Night," "Honey Love," "White Christmas," "What'cha Gonna Do" — raw, gospel-blooded sides that are some of the form's foundational records. The post-McPhatter group reorganized by manager George Treadwell, fronted by Ben E. King and later Rudy Lewis and Johnny Moore, cut "There Goes My Baby," "Save the Last Dance for Me," "Up on the Roof," "Under the Boardwalk" — which are barely doo-wop at all and belong to the Brill Building / uptown soul story. The 1958 Atlantic LP *Rockin' & Driftin'* compiles McPhatter-era material; the fuller documentation is on Atlantic R&B box and on dedicated McPhatter-era retrospectives. Scaruffi sharply prefers the McPhatter Drifters — gospel rawness over uptown polish — and would rank only the McPhatter-era material here. Fantano (extrapolated) is more agnostic and might give some weight to "There Goes My Baby" as the moment doo-wop dissolved into soul, but the gospel intensity of McPhatter's lead on "Money Honey" is too obviously the better artistic peak to argue with. The entry is for the McPhatter-era Drifters specifically; the Ben E. King group belongs in the soul volume.

##### 5. The Moonglows — *Look! It's the Moonglows* (Chess, 1959)

The Moonglows are Scaruffi's other haunting-doo-wop favorite, and *Look! It's the Moonglows* — the 1959 Chess LP, assembled from sides cut between 1954 and 1958 under Alan Freed's quasi-management (Freed took a writer credit on "Sincerely," a long-standing scandal) — is the closest the group came to a coherent album document. The original lineup of Harvey Fuqua, Bobby Lester, Pete Graves, and Prentiss Barnes invented the "blow-harmony" technique on "Sincerely," "Most of All," "We Go Together," "See Saw," and "Ten Commandments of Love" — a vocal effect where the background voices blow rather than sing the harmonies, creating a foggy, slightly disturbed atmosphere that is doo-wop's most distinctive sonic signature. Bobby Lester's lead voice has a tremor in it that is half-gospel, half-haunted; Harvey Fuqua's bass underpinnings are unusually melodic. Scaruffi rates the Moonglows among the form's top two or three groups specifically because the blow-harmony texture has no precedent and no descendants — it is a genuine sonic invention. Fantano (extrapolated) would respond to the production strangeness for the same reasons he responds to Phil Spector and Joe Meek productions: the technique is so clearly authored that the records have a recognizable signature rather than a generic vocal-group sound. Best documented today on the Chess Doo Wop anthologies and on Rhino's *The Doo Wop Box*.

##### 6. The Platters — *The Platters* (Mercury, 1956) / *Encore of Golden Hits* (Mercury, 1960)

The Platters are the slick-uptown pole that Scaruffi is cool on and that the form's commercial dominance is built around. From 1955 to 1960, with Tony Williams on lead (Williams's classically-trained tenor is the group's signal), David Lynch, Paul Robi, Herb Reed, and Zola Taylor as the singing-and-pretending-the-Crew-Cuts-don't-exist quintet — managed and largely written by Buck Ram — the Platters cut "Only You (And You Alone)," "The Great Pretender," "My Prayer," "Twilight Time," and "Smoke Gets in Your Eyes," charting on white pop radio in a way no Black doo-wop group did before them. *The Platters* (Mercury, 1956) is the first LP; *Encore of Golden Hits* (Mercury, 1960) is the canonical period compilation. Scaruffi files them as proficient but commercially compromised — the orchestral arrangements (string sections, brushed jazz drums) move them away from doo-wop's vocal-group center toward easy-listening — and would rank them lower than this slot suggests. Fantano (extrapolated) is more skeptical still; the Platters are exactly the kind of slick legacy act that the Fantano framing would identify as nostalgia object rather than living art. But Tony Williams's lead on "The Great Pretender" is unanswerable, and "Smoke Gets in Your Eyes" is one of the great vocal performances of the period in any genre. They survive the debate at number six on Williams's voice and on commercial-historical weight, against both critics' instincts.

##### 7. Frankie Lymon & The Teenagers — *The Teenagers Featuring Frankie Lymon* (Gee, 1956)

Frankie Lymon was thirteen years old when "Why Do Fools Fall in Love" hit in 1956, fronting a Harlem teenage group (Herman Santiago, Jimmy Merchant, Joe Negroni, Sherman Garnes — note the Puerto Rican members; this is one of the form's first integrated lineups) on the Gee label out of George Goldner's New York operation. The 1956 Gee LP *The Teenagers Featuring Frankie Lymon* is the only period album document and is one of the earliest rock-and-roll LPs by a Black act. Beyond the title hit, "I Want You to Be My Girl," "I Promise to Remember," "The ABC's of Love," and "Goody Goody" are on the standard compilations. Lymon's voice — a clear, almost vibratoless boy soprano with adult phrasing — is the technical wonder of the form; he died of a heroin overdose at 25 in 1968 having long since lost the voice when it broke. Scaruffi acknowledges Lymon as a phenomenon but is wary of the novelty framing — he prefers the haunting groups to the prodigy hit-machines. Fantano (extrapolated) would be drawn to Lymon for the same reason Fantano is drawn to other doomed boy-genius figures (his treatment of Daniel Johnston, of pre-fame Lil Peep): the biographical arc is too sharp to ignore. Both agree the album survives mostly because of "Why Do Fools Fall in Love" but that the song is enough.

##### 8. The Five Satins — *In the Still of the Nite* (Ember, 1957) / Canonical Compilations

The Five Satins exist because of one song. "In the Still of the Nite," cut by Fred Parris and the group (the original Five Satins were New Haven, Connecticut, not Harlem or Philadelphia — the form's geographic spread mattered) reportedly in a church basement (the Saint Bernadette's church basement story is a doo-wop folklore staple; the recording location of the original 1956 session is generally cited as such, but I flag the venue claim because doo-wop oral history is unreliable), released on the small Standord label in 1956 and picked up by Ember for national distribution, is one of the form's two or three foundational records. The "shoo-doo-shoo-be-doo" backing vocal is one of the most recognized nonsense-syllable hooks in American popular music. The 1957 Ember LP *In the Still of the Nite* (often titled with variant spellings) collected the title song with weaker follow-ups; the group never matched it. Scaruffi gives the song extraordinary weight and the album modest weight and ranks the group accordingly. Fantano (extrapolated) would identify "In the Still of the Nite" as a one-shot masterpiece in the way "Be My Baby" is a one-shot masterpiece — the surrounding catalog is unnecessary but the song defines the form. Best heard today on Rhino's *The Doo Wop Box* and on the Ember Records compilations.

##### 9. The Penguins / The Cadillacs / Various — Singles Compilations

This slot is a deliberate compression of three groups whose work overlaps temporally and structurally and whose individual album outputs do not justify standalone slots. The Penguins — Cleveland Duncan, Curtis Williams, Dexter Tisby, Bruce Tate — recorded "Earth Angel (Will You Be Mine)" for Dootone Records in Los Angeles in late 1954; it became one of the form's two or three defining ballads and one of the first Black doo-wop sides to cross substantially to white radio. The Cadillacs — Earl Carroll lead, on Josie Records out of New York — cut "Speedoo" in 1955, an uptempo novelty that brought the comic-character mode (later perfected by the Coasters) into doo-wop. The Channels, the Cleftones, the Harptones, the Spaniels (whose "Goodnight Sweetheart, Goodnight" on Vee-Jay closed thousands of dances and high school proms), the Five Keys (one of the form's most musically sophisticated groups, with a strong gospel inheritance) all belong here in spirit. The honest representation of this layer of the canon is via Rhino's *The Doo Wop Box*, the Atlantic R&B box, and the various Chess and Ace UK doo-wop anthologies. Scaruffi spreads his enthusiasm across this middle tier without strongly preferring one group. Fantano (extrapolated) has no documented preferences here; the extrapolation is that he would treat them as the Black-originator base that the more famous groups (Platters, Lymon, later Drifters) extracted commercial value from.

##### 10. Dion & The Belmonts — *Presenting Dion & The Belmonts* (Laurie, 1959)

Dion DiMucci and the Belmonts — Carlo Mastrangelo, Fred Milano, Angelo D'Aleo, from the Belmont neighborhood of the Bronx — are the canonical Italian-American doo-wop group and the form's most successful white act. *Presenting Dion & The Belmonts* (Laurie, 1959) includes "I Wonder Why," "No One Knows," "A Teenager in Love" (the Doc Pomus / Mort Shuman song), and "Where or When." Dion's lead voice has a nasal Bronx tenor that is half R&B, half Italian-American street, and it became one of the recognizable sounds of late-period doo-wop. Scaruffi is skeptical of the Italian-American doo-wop wave — he reads it as the form's commercial-decline phase, when the originating Black groups had stopped charting and white teen audiences wanted a face-they-recognized version — and would rank Dion lower than this slot. Fantano (extrapolated) is similarly skeptical for similar reasons and would probably argue for the Skyliners ("Since I Don't Have You," 1959, from Pittsburgh, the white group with the most genuine claim to having added something) over Dion. Both critics, however, concede that "A Teenager in Love" and "I Wonder Why" are real songs and that the album survives. This entry comes in tenth specifically because the white-doo-wop layer is part of the genre's honest history even when the critics are cool on it. The Skyliners and the Dell-Vikings (whose "Come Go With Me" was an early integrated-lineup hit) are the strongest competing claims for this slot, sent below to honorable mentions and to the coin-flips.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

Three picks were irreconcilable after six rounds. They went to coin-flip.

**Coin-flip 1: Frankie Lymon & The Teenagers vs. The Spaniels.** Scaruffi pulled toward The Spaniels — James "Pookie" Hudson's lead on "Goodnight Sweetheart, Goodnight" (Vee-Jay, 1954) is one of the form's most quietly devastating performances and the song is foundational to doo-wop's ballad mode. Fantano (extrapolated) pulled toward Lymon — the biographical arc and the boy-soprano oddity are exactly the kind of figure he would respond to. Coin came up Lymon. The Spaniels move to honorable mentions, with the note that their absence from the top 10 is a real loss; "Goodnight Sweetheart, Goodnight" is arguably more important than several included songs.

**Coin-flip 2: The Platters vs. The Five Keys.** Both critics were lukewarm on the Platters (slick, white-pop-crossover, orchestral) and warm on the Five Keys (gospel-rooted, musically sophisticated, less famous). Scaruffi wanted to remove the Platters on artistic grounds; Fantano (extrapolated) wanted to keep them because Tony Williams's vocal performance on "The Great Pretender" is non-negotiable as a singing event. Coin came up Platters. The Five Keys move to honorable mentions with active regret — by pure aesthetic logic they should be in and the Platters should be out, but the Platters' commercial-historical weight made the keep argument win the coin.

**Coin-flip 3: Dion & The Belmonts vs. The Skyliners.** Scaruffi argued the Skyliners — "Since I Don't Have You" (1959) is the strongest single record by a white doo-wop group and Jimmy Beaumont's lead is more interesting than Dion's. Fantano (extrapolated) argued Dion — the body of work is larger, the Belmonts contributed more to the form than the Skyliners did, and Dion's later solo career (the more interesting Dion is the post-Belmonts blues-folk Dion of "Abraham, Martin and John" and the 1968 self-titled LP, but that's outside this volume's scope) gives the act a longer artistic life. Coin came up Dion. The Skyliners move to honorable mentions; "Since I Don't Have You" is one of the records anyone serious about the form has to know.

#### Honorable Mentions

**The Skyliners**, **The Spaniels**, **The Five Keys** — see coin-flips above. All three have legitimate claims to top-10 slots; their exclusion is a function of slot scarcity, not of artistic shortfall.

**The Orioles** (with Sonny Til on lead, Jubilee Records, late 1940s into early '50s) are the form's true originators — "It's Too Soon to Know" (1948) is arguably the first record that sounds recognizably doo-wop. They were excluded from the top 10 on the grounds that the pre-1953 vocal-group sides belong to a transitional R&B period that the term "doo-wop" was retroactively applied to. Honest historians would put the Orioles higher. Scaruffi acknowledges them as foundational.

**The Ravens** (Jimmy Ricks on lead bass, late 1940s) are the other foundational pre-doo-wop vocal group; "Ol' Man River" (1947) is the canonical pre-history. Same exclusion logic as the Orioles.

**The Cleftones**, **The Channels** ("The Closer You Are," 1956, is one of the form's prettier ballads), **The Harptones** (the New York group with the underrated Willie Winfield lead), **The Clovers** (Atlantic act, more R&B than doo-wop but adjacent — "Love Potion No. 9" is technically post-doo-wop), **The Dell-Vikings** ("Come Go With Me," 1956, was an early integrated-lineup hit on Dot/Mercury).

**The Crests** — "16 Candles" (Coed, 1958) with Johnny Maestro on lead — are the other major Italian-American-led doo-wop act (with mixed-race membership). Strong claim that didn't quite make it.

**The Marcels** — "Blue Moon" (Colpix, 1961, the "bom-ba-ba-bom" intro) is the form's last great novelty hit and one of the most recognizable doo-wop recordings, but the group's catalog beyond it is thin.

**The Diamonds**, **The Crew-Cuts**, **The Four Seasons** — three white groups frequently miscategorized as doo-wop. The Diamonds and Crew-Cuts were white-cover acts whose business model was charting white-radio versions of Black doo-wop hits ("Little Darlin'," "Sh-Boom"); both critics regard them as outside the canon. The Four Seasons emerged from doo-wop into a post-doo-wop teen-pop / Brill Building zone after 1962; they belong to a different chapter.

**Compilations consulted as honest sources for this essay:** Rhino's *The Doo Wop Box* (1993) and *The Doo Wop Box II* (1996) are the spine. *Atlantic Rhythm and Blues 1947-1974* (Atlantic / Rhino, eight-volume box) is the second spine for Atlantic-roster groups (Drifters, Clovers, Coasters). Ace Records UK has issued reliable single-label retrospectives across multiple doo-wop labels (the *Doo-Wop / Rhythm and Blues* Ace series, with specific titles varying — I cite the imprint rather than fabricated titles I cannot verify). Bear Family has done multi-disc retrospectives on several major acts. Chess and Vee-Jay have had their vocal-group sides anthologized multiple times under various titles (I avoid citing specific Chess doo-wop compilation titles I am not confident about). The most reliable rule for a listener building a doo-wop collection in 2026 is: start with the two Rhino boxes, then add Ace UK single-label volumes for whichever labels (Gee, End, Vee-Jay, Herald, Old Town) speak to you.

**A final honest note on the canon problem.** Doo-wop is the genre where the album-ranking exercise is most strained. The form's living art is in three-minute 45 RPM singles played on car radios in 1958, and the form's heard-today version is in curated compilations assembled in the 1990s. Both Scaruffi (in his measured way) and a hypothetical Fantano (extrapolated, with stated uncertainty) would push back on the ranking framework before agreeing to it. The 10 entries above are honest within the framework's limits. Listeners who want the form should go to the Rhino *Doo Wop Box* first, read Anthony J. Gribin and Matthew M. Schiff's *Doo-Wop: The Forgotten Third of Rock 'n' Roll* (1992) for context, and treat the rest as footnotes.

---

### Early R&B / Rhythm and Blues
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

Early R&B is the bridge era — late 1940s through the early 1960s, after jump blues cooled into something more song-oriented and before Stax and Motown rewired the form into soul. It's the period when the 12-bar blues got a horn section, a backbeat, and a vocal personality that could sell ballads to teenagers and adults at the same time. Ray Charles is the gravitational center; Sam Cooke is the moral and melodic counterweight; everyone else orbits between them.

The critical problem is that this is an *era of singles*, not albums. Most of the canonical work was cut for 78s and 45s. The "albums" that define the period are either (a) actual LPs that artists made once the format mattered, like Ray Charles's late-Atlantic concept records and Sam Cooke's *Night Beat*, or (b) compilations assembled retrospectively from singles sessions. Any honest top-10 has to mix both, and we've tried to flag which is which.

Scaruffi is unusually present on this material — he reveres Ray Charles's Atlantic period, calls *Live at the Harlem Square Club* one of the great live records, and treats Sam Cooke's secular work as the moment American popular song stopped being entertainment and became art. He's cooler on the doo-wop-adjacent groups and the showbiz end (Jackie Wilson, Clyde McPhatter as a frontman) and warmer on the deeper blues-rooted figures (Big Mama Thornton, Bobby Bland).

Fantano is the harder problem. He has effectively *zero* on-record commentary on the 1950s R&B canon. He's reviewed reissues and retrospectives tangentially, and his general aesthetic — preference for live energy over studio polish, suspicion of crossover smoothness, reverence for Black vocal traditions — suggests how he'd lean, but anything specific is **EXTRAPOLATION** and is flagged as such throughout. We've tried to be conservative: where Fantano's framework genuinely predicts a position, we say so; where it's a guess, we admit it.

The debate ran six rounds. Major fault lines:

1. **Ray Charles album count.** Scaruffi wants three Charles LPs in the top 10 (*The Genius of Ray Charles*, *Modern Sounds in Country & Western Music*, and a live record). Fantano-extrapolated pushed back on the crossover *Modern Sounds* as too smooth. We kept two.
2. **Sam Cooke studio vs live.** Both critics gravitate to *Live at the Harlem Square Club*; the question was whether *Night Beat* or *Ain't That Good News* gets the second slot. Scaruffi favors *Night Beat* (the after-hours, blues-leaning one); Fantano-extrapolated would likely agree based on his preference for darker, less polished work. *Night Beat* won.
3. **The compilation question.** Should retrospective compilations of singles-era artists (Ruth Brown, LaVern Baker, Hank Ballard) count? We allowed them but only where the singles cohere into a body of work and only citing real, released compilations (Atlantic *R&B Years*, Specialty Singles, Chess box sets) — no invented titles.
4. **Chuck Berry.** Goes in the Early Rock'n'Roll file, not here. His R&B-leaning sides are the borderline case and we noted them in honorable mentions rather than the top 10.
5. **Etta James timing.** *At Last!* (1960) is technically pre-Motown/Stax soul but already pointing forward. It belongs here as the closing bracket of the era.
6. **Solomon Burke.** Scaruffi loves the early Atlantic Burke as the literal hinge into soul. Fantano-extrapolated unclear. Compromise: honorable mention, since his peak Atlantic LP is genuinely the first *soul* album and arguably belongs in the next file.

Three irreconcilables went to coin flip. Results in the Resolutions section.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Ray Charles — *The Genius of Ray Charles* (Atlantic, 1959)

Scaruffi calls this the album where Ray Charles invented the modern Black popular vocalist — not because the singing is better than what he was doing on the singles ("What'd I Say," "I Got a Woman") but because the *frame* finally caught up to him. Side one is big band, side two is strings. Both sides treat Charles's voice as the instrument that decides what a song *means*, not just how it sounds. "Let the Good Times Roll," "Don't Let the Sun Catch You Crying," "Am I Blue" — these aren't covers, they're rereadings. The blues phrasing eats the standards alive.

The album matters historically because it was the first time a Black R&B singer was given the orchestral apparatus previously reserved for Sinatra-tier white pop. The decision wasn't aesthetic, it was political — Atlantic's Ahmet Ertegun and Jerry Wexler (producers credited on the project, though we won't pretend to know who tracked which side) were betting that Charles could carry a "prestige" LP. He could. The bet paid, and the precedent it set — that an R&B artist could make a record *about* American song, not just inside it — opened the door for everything Cooke, Aretha, and the Motown vocalists later did.

Fantano-extrapolated note: Fantano has never reviewed this and his preference for rawer, less arranged Black music suggests he'd respect it without loving it. But the album's structural ambition — using two distinct ensembles to argue two halves of one personality — is exactly the kind of conceptual integrity he tends to reward. We're scoring it as mutual #1 by acclamation: Scaruffi's reverence is documented, Fantano's hypothetical respect is the safest extrapolation in the whole list.

The record is also the answer to the question "when did the album format start to matter for Black popular music." Before this, it didn't, really. After this, it did.

##### 2. Sam Cooke — *Live at the Harlem Square Club, 1963* (RCA, recorded 1963, released 1985)

Scaruffi is on record calling this one of the greatest live albums ever made. The story is famous: RCA recorded Cooke working a Black nightclub crowd in Miami, heard how *unsmooth* he was — sweat, rasp, audience screaming over him, gospel-derived holler phrases bleeding into the secular hits — and shelved it for twenty-two years because it didn't match the supper-club image they'd built. When it finally came out in 1985 it rewrote Cooke's reputation overnight.

The performance is the case for Cooke as a sanctified R&B singer first and a crossover balladeer second. "Bring It On Home to Me," "Cupid," "Twistin' the Night Away," "Having a Party" — songs that on the studio versions are tidy and radio-shaped — get pulled inside-out. The band is tight in the way a road band is tight (not in the way a session band is tight), the audience is loud, and Cooke spends the set climbing the rafters of his own range. The contrast with the contemporaneous *Sam Cooke at the Copa* — same artist, same year, white audience, dinner jackets — is one of the most explicit demonstrations on record of the double consciousness a Black performer was navigating in 1963.

Fantano-extrapolated: of all the records on this list, this is the one we're most confident he'd love. The aesthetic — live, raw, gospel-rooted, the audience as collaborator, a recovered document that overturns received wisdom — maps directly onto every one of his actually-stated preferences. We're calling this his #1 by extrapolation while flagging that it's a guess. **EXTRAPOLATED.**

The record's posthumous release also means it functions as a kind of curated truth-telling: Cooke didn't choose to release it, RCA chose not to, and history chose to anyway. That arc gives it more critical weight than a record an artist actively shaped.

##### 3. Ray Charles — *Modern Sounds in Country and Western Music* (ABC-Paramount, 1962)

The argument-starter. Charles, having left Atlantic for ABC and a better royalty deal, made a record of country and western standards arranged with strings, choirs, and big band — "I Can't Stop Loving You," "You Don't Know Me," "Born to Lose," "You Win Again." It was the bestselling album of his career and one of the bestselling LPs of 1962 overall.

Scaruffi reveres it as a Trojan horse: Charles smuggled the country songbook into Black American culture and Black phrasing into the country songbook, and the resulting hybrid was something neither genre had a name for. The political subtext — a Black artist at the height of the civil rights era covering songs by white Southern songwriters and outselling everyone — is impossible to ignore. Charles wasn't softening; he was *colonizing*.

The Fantano-extrapolated objection is the obvious one: the arrangements are lush in a way that can read as middlebrow if you're hostile to the era's orchestral pop conventions. Strings, the Jack Halloran Singers cooing behind him, a production glossiness that's a long way from the Atlantic horn-section grit. We considered this seriously and kept the record at #3 anyway, because the *vocal performance* is uncompromised — Charles is doing the same thing he did on "Drown in My Own Tears," he's just doing it over different chord changes — and because the historical importance is too large to argue around. **EXTRAPOLATED Fantano objection noted.**

If you only know one Ray Charles album from the era, it's probably this one. That's by design.

##### 4. Sam Cooke — *Night Beat* (RCA, 1963)

The other Cooke entry, and the studio counterweight to *Harlem Square*. *Night Beat* is the after-hours record: Cooke working through ballads and blues at low volume with a small combo, the band hushed, the singing almost conversational. "Lost and Lookin'," "Mean Old World," "Little Red Rooster," "Trouble Blues" — material that gives him room to be a blues singer rather than a hitmaker.

Scaruffi treats it as proof that Cooke could have been the great American blues balladeer if he'd chosen to. The arrangements are dark and uncluttered, the tempos slow, the vocal placed unusually close. It's the most introspective record of his career.

Fantano-extrapolated would likely prefer this to most of Cooke's other studio work for the same reasons Scaruffi does: minimal arrangement, vocal in the foreground, blues structure, no crossover sweetening. We're confident on this one within the limits of extrapolation. **EXTRAPOLATED but well-supported.**

*Night Beat* is also the answer to the question "what would Cooke have sounded like if he'd lived past 1964 and into the era of Memphis soul." Stripped, intimate, blues-leaning — basically the template Bobby Womack and Otis Redding would extend on their own ballad work. The continuity from this record into late-60s soul is almost embarrassingly direct.

##### 5. Ray Charles — *Ray Charles at Newport* (Atlantic, 1958)

The live document of the Atlantic period, recorded at the Newport Jazz Festival. The set is short — eight tracks — but the band is the great late-50s Charles band with David "Fathead" Newman on tenor, and the performances of "The Right Time," "I Got a Woman," "A Fool for You," and "Talkin' 'Bout You" are the definitive versions of those songs. Charles is leading from the piano, the horn section is biting, and the audience is doing the thing audiences only do for performers who have already become legends.

This is the record where you can hear the gospel architecture under everything — call-and-response, the rhythmic feel of a Black church on a Sunday morning transplanted onto secular material. Scaruffi treats Newport as the bridge between Charles's singles-era genius and the album-era genius of *The Genius of Ray Charles*. Both critics-by-extrapolation would agree this is one of the indispensable live R&B documents.

We considered slotting *Ray Charles in Person* (1960, Atlanta) here instead, since Scaruffi has at times preferred it for the Black audience and rawer feel. The decision came down to availability of consensus: Newport is more widely cited, easier to point to, and the band is at peak ferocity. *In Person* gets the honorable mention. **EXTRAPOLATED Fantano position; Scaruffi position documented.**

##### 6. Etta James — *At Last!* (Argo/Chess, 1960)

The closing bracket of the early-R&B era and the opening bracket of soul. James was a Chess artist working with Riley Hampton's string arrangements, and the album is structurally the same kind of project as *The Genius of Ray Charles* — Black R&B voice plus orchestral apparatus, treating the singer as the prestige object.

What separates *At Last!* from the Charles record is the depth of the *desire* in the vocal. James sings "At Last," "A Sunday Kind of Love," "Stormy Weather," "Trust in Me," "I Just Want to Make Love to You" with a sexual gravity that no other vocalist of the era — Cooke, Charles, Aretha (still at Columbia in 1960) — was bringing to material that smooth. The record was the first time a young Black woman was given a top-tier orchestral pop production *and* allowed to sing about wanting somebody like she actually wanted them.

Scaruffi respects James without elevating her to the Charles/Cooke tier. Fantano-extrapolated would likely respect the record for the same reasons he respects Amy Winehouse — a young Black-tradition woman singer working at a level of emotional commitment that the polite arrangements can barely contain. **EXTRAPOLATED.**

*At Last!* is also the moment when R&B as a commercial category started becoming culturally indistinguishable from soul. The next time a record this controlled and this hungry came out of a Black-owned American label, it was a Motown record.

##### 7. Various Artists — *Atlantic R&B 1947–1974, Vol. 2 (1952–1955)* (Atlantic compilation)

The compilation entry. Atlantic Records was the single most important institution in early R&B — Ertegun, Wexler, Tom Dowd, the artists they signed (Ruth Brown, LaVern Baker, Joe Turner, Clyde McPhatter, the Drifters, Ray Charles, eventually Solomon Burke and Aretha) — and their multi-volume *Atlantic R&B* compilation series is the standard way to enter the singles canon.

Volume 2 covers 1952–1955, which is roughly the peak of the early-R&B singles era: Ruth Brown's "Mama He Treats Your Daughter Mean" and "5-10-15 Hours," Joe Turner's "Honey Hush" and "Shake, Rattle and Roll," LaVern Baker's "Tweedle Dee" and "Jim Dandy," Clyde McPhatter and the Drifters' "Money Honey," the Clovers, the Cardinals. This is the music that was actually on Black jukeboxes from Memphis to Harlem in the years before rock'n'roll became a marketing category.

Scaruffi's framework treats the singles-era artists as collectively important rather than individually canonical — none of Ruth Brown, LaVern Baker, or Joe Turner made a definitive *album*, but the body of work they cut for Atlantic between 1951 and 1955 is collectively as important as any of the individual masterpieces above. The compilation is the honest way to acknowledge that.

Fantano-extrapolated has no position on these singles artists and probably hasn't engaged with this comp. We're including it on Scaruffi's behalf and on historical grounds, flagged. **EXTRAPOLATED Fantano agnostic.**

##### 8. Big Mama Thornton — *In Europe* (Arhoolie, 1966)

Cut in London (Wessex Studios, October 20, 1965) during the American Folk Blues Festival tour, this is Thornton with a Chicago blues band — Buddy Guy on guitar, Fred McDowell on slide, Walter "Shakey" Horton on harmonica, Eddie Boyd on piano/organ, Jimmy Lee Robinson on electric bass, Fred Below on drums — running through her singles-era material ("Hound Dog," which she cut in 1952 four years before Elvis), Willie Mae blues, and standards. The album technically falls outside the strict 1950s window but it documents an artist whose recording career was definitively a late-40s/50s phenomenon.

Scaruffi takes Thornton seriously as the link between the classic blueswomen (Bessie Smith, Ma Rainey) and the R&B-era shouters who came after. Her voice is unmediated power — none of the Cooke-style finesse, none of the Charles-style architectural cleverness, just lung capacity and conviction. The Peacock singles she cut in Houston in 1952–53 are the canonical version of her work, but they exist only as scattered singles and compilations; *In Europe* is the closest thing to a proper album that captures what she actually sounded like.

Fantano-extrapolated: probably warm. Thornton fits his profile of artists whose historical importance was sublimated by white covers (Elvis, Joplin), and his sympathy with reclamation projects makes this an easy bet — but it remains a bet. **EXTRAPOLATED.**

If you're looking for a single-disc Thornton compilation instead, MCA/Peacock's *Hound Dog: The Peacock Recordings* is the standard reference for the original 1952–57 sides. Either is defensible; *In Europe* wins on coherence.

##### 9. Bobby "Blue" Bland — *Two Steps from the Blues* (Duke, 1961)

The most consistently underrated record of the era and the one that most directly anticipates Memphis soul. Bland was a Duke Records artist working with arranger and trumpeter Joe Scott, and the album collects ten Duke singles cut in the late 1950s and 1960 — "I Pity the Fool," "Lead Me On," "I'll Take Care of You," "Cry, Cry, Cry," "St. James Infirmary," and the title track.

What separates Bland from the rest of the early-R&B field is the *restraint*. He doesn't shout, he doesn't testify, he doesn't dazzle. He phrases like a deeply controlled jazz singer who happens to be working over a blues band, and the band — Wayne Bennett on guitar throughout, John "Jabo" Starks on drums (later the JBs' co-drummer with Clyde Stubblefield), Hamp Simmons on bass, Teddy Reynolds on piano, Joe Scott leading the horn section — is doing the chord-substitution work that would, two or three years later, become recognizable as the Memphis soul rhythm-section sound. You can hear directly from Bland into Stax (Otis Redding cited him repeatedly) and into the smoother Atlantic soul of Solomon Burke.

Scaruffi rates Bland highly; his sense is that Bland is the missing link between Ray Charles's blues-rooted phrasing and what Otis would do at Stax. Fantano-extrapolated has no documented engagement with Bland, but the album's restraint, depth, and influence on artists he *does* engage with (the Stax canon) makes it likely he'd respect it. **EXTRAPOLATED.**

*Two Steps from the Blues* is the album people who study the era discover and then never stop arguing should be more famous than it is. Part of the obscurity is structural: Duke Records, run by Don Robey out of Houston, never had Atlantic's reach into the white press, so Bland's reputation has always lived more in musicians' word-of-mouth than in mainstream rock criticism. The album was reissued enough times by enough imprints that there's no canonical edition; pick any version that includes the original ten-song running order and you have the record.

The lyrical content is also worth noting. Bland sings almost entirely about romantic damage and self-knowledge — "I'll Take Care of You" is a vow, "I Pity the Fool" is a warning, "Lead Me On" is a confession — and the cumulative effect across the LP is of a man working through a single emotional problem from ten angles. That kind of thematic cohesion was rare in 1961 R&B and is one of the reasons the record survives.

##### 10. Hank Ballard & The Midnighters — *Their Greatest Juke Box Hits* (King, 1958)

The proto-rock and proto-funk entry. Ballard wrote and originally recorded "The Twist" in late 1958 — the single was issued in 1959 as the B-side of "Teardrops on Your Letter" — and Chubby Checker's cover, recorded in 1960 and #1 on the Hot 100 that September, became the cultural phenomenon, but Ballard's original is the actual record. He also cut the "Annie" trilogy — "Work With Me, Annie," "Annie Had a Baby," "Annie's Aunt Fannie" — which were so sexually explicit by 1954 standards that they got banned from major radio while still selling massively in the Black market.

The King compilation is the rough-and-tumble counter-canon to the Atlantic uptown sound. Ballard's records are *funkier* in the literal sense — a harder backbeat, a less polished horn arrangement, vocals that sit closer to shouting than to crooning. He's the late-50s figure most directly anticipating James Brown (King label-mate, and Brown explicitly cited Ballard as foundational).

Scaruffi takes Ballard seriously on rhythmic grounds — the King sides are part of how R&B drum and bass parts hardened into the syncopation that would become funk by 1965. Fantano-extrapolated: Ballard's lineage to James Brown (whom Fantano has engaged with) makes this a plausible inclusion, but it remains a guess. **EXTRAPOLATED.**

We considered an Ike Turner-with-Tina entry here instead (the early Sue Records sides) but settled on Ballard because the rhythmic-historical case is cleaner: this is where the Black backbeat got mean enough to become funk.

The Cincinnati King operation deserves a sentence on its own. Syd Nathan's label was the home of Ballard, James Brown, Wynonie Harris, Bull Moose Jackson, and a deep roster of country and R&B singles artists. King's house sound — fewer strings, drier rhythm section, more emphasis on the in-the-pocket horn punch than on Atlantic-style polish — is the through-line between late-40s jump blues and mid-60s soul, and Ballard's records sit dead center in that lineage. If you want to hear what an R&B record sounded like in 1958 when nobody was trying to chase the Atlantic crossover formula, this is the document.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

Three positions were irreconcilable between Scaruffi's documented preferences and Fantano-extrapolated framework. We resolved each with a coin flip and disclose the results:

**Slot for #5 — Ray Charles live album: *Ray Charles at Newport* vs *Ray Charles in Person.*** Scaruffi has at various points preferred *In Person* for the Black-audience setting and rawer recording. Fantano-extrapolated would likely also prefer *In Person* on raw-vs-polished grounds. But the wider critical consensus and accessibility favor *Newport*. Coin flip went to *Newport*. *In Person* drops to honorable mentions.

**Slot for #7 — singles compilation: *Atlantic R&B 1952-1955* vs *The Specialty Story* (Specialty box covering Lloyd Price, Larry Williams, Little Richard's R&B sides).** Scaruffi leans Atlantic on label-importance grounds; Fantano-extrapolated genuinely indeterminate. Coin flip went to Atlantic. The Specialty material gets a nod in honorable mentions.

**Slot for #10 — proto-funk entry: Hank Ballard vs early Ike & Tina Turner (Sue Records 1960–62, "A Fool in Love," "It's Gonna Work Out Fine").** Both critics-by-position would have defensible cases. Coin flip went to Ballard on grounds that the King label compilation is a cleaner album-shaped artifact than the scattered early Ike & Tina singles. Ike & Tina honorable mention below.

#### Honorable Mentions

- **Ray Charles — *Ray Charles in Person* (Atlantic, 1960).** Lost the coin flip to *Newport*. Recorded at an Atlanta concert, all-Black audience, rougher sound. Scaruffi's preferred Charles live record.
- **Solomon Burke — *Rock 'n Soul* (Atlantic, 1964).** The hinge album where R&B becomes soul. Bumped to the Soul file because the next-era Stax/Atlantic soul lineage starts here.
- **Sam Cooke — *Ain't That Good News* (RCA, 1964).** Cooke's last studio album, containing "A Change Is Gonna Come." Bumped on the grounds that *Live at Harlem Square* and *Night Beat* already cover the studio/live duality and "Change" is the song carrying it rather than the album as a whole.
- **Jackie Wilson — *Mr. Excitement!* (Rhino box, retrospective).** The standard Wilson compilation. Held back because Scaruffi is cool on Wilson's showbiz tendencies and Fantano-extrapolated unclear.
- **Lloyd Price / Specialty Singles — *The Specialty Story.*** New Orleans R&B and the bridge into rock'n'roll. Lost the comp coin flip.
- **Ruth Brown — *Miss Rhythm* (Atlantic singles compilations).** Atlantic's first female star and definitionally important; held back because her material is largely covered by the *Atlantic R&B* comp at #7.
- **LaVern Baker — *Soul on Fire: The Best of LaVern Baker* (Atlantic compilation).** Same logic.
- **Ike & Tina Turner — early Sue Records sides.** Lost the proto-funk coin flip to Ballard.
- **Clyde McPhatter & The Drifters — early Atlantic sides.** Foundational vocal-group R&B; bumped because the doo-wop-leaning material may belong in a separate vocal-groups file.
- **Faye Adams, Ivory Joe Hunter, Joe Turner solo compilations.** All real, all important, all defensible — but each is more useful inside an Atlantic comp than as a standalone LP, and we've kept the list LP-shaped where possible.
- **Chuck Berry's R&B-leaning Chess sides.** Bumped to the Early Rock'n'Roll file where his rock'n'roll material lives. He belongs in both files conceptually; we've put him in one.

---

*Notes on method: Fantano's positions throughout this file are extrapolated from his general aesthetic — preference for live energy over studio polish, suspicion of crossover smoothness, sympathy with reclamation of underrecognized Black artists — and have been flagged inline. He has not, as of writing, published direct commentary on the 1950s R&B canon. Scaruffi's positions are documented in his Ray Charles and Sam Cooke entries and in his general History of Rock Music. All recording venues, session dates, and personnel cited above have been kept generic where unverified; producers cited (Ahmet Ertegun, Jerry Wexler at Atlantic; Joe Scott at Duke; Riley Hampton on Etta James) are documented. Compilation titles cited are real Atlantic, Specialty, Chess, MCA/Peacock, and Rhino releases.*

---

### Motown / Northern Soul
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

A quick honesty note before we begin: "Northern Soul" is not a sound, it is a reception. It refers to the British dance-floor cult that, starting in the late 1960s, fetishized obscure American soul 45s — many of them on Motown subsidiaries, many of them on labels Motown's gravity outshone. The scene's canon is a 7-inch canon, not an album canon. So this entry is really about Motown proper: the Detroit assembly line, the Funk Brothers' uncredited groove (James Jamerson on bass, Benny Benjamin and Richard "Pistol" Allen and Uriel Jones on drums, Earl Van Dyke on piano, Robert White and Joe Messina and Eddie Willis on guitar, Jack Ashford on percussion — names worth saying once even if the rest of this essay falls back on the collective shorthand), the singles-first mid-60s, and the artist-auteur album era that Berry Gordy was eventually dragged into by Marvin Gaye and Stevie Wonder. Where a Northern Soul-adjacent record fits the album frame, it shows up. Otherwise: Hitsville.

#### Debate Summary

The Scaruffi top 15 and the Fantano top 15 agreed almost completely at the top. Both opened with *What's Going On*. Both had *Innervisions* and *Songs in the Key of Life* in the top five. Both included *Talking Book*, *Fulfillingness' First Finale*, *Here, My Dear*, and *Let's Get It On*. The argument was never whether Stevie's classic period belongs — it was how much of it belongs, and at the expense of what.

Scaruffi treats the Holland-Dozier-Holland singles era as commerce. He'd cut the Supremes, the Four Tops, and Smokey Robinson entirely if allowed, on the grounds that the LP was an afterthought and the genuine artifact is the 7-inch. He pushed *Music of My Mind* — Stevie's first album as a full self-produced auteur — over more polished later work. He wanted *Cloud Nine* (Temptations) for psychedelic-soul credibility, and he was firm on *I Want You* as Marvin's most underrated album.

Fantano wanted to honor the singles legacy somehow, even if he agreed the LPs were uneven. He pushed a Temptations compilation as a structural fix — comps are legitimate album-format objects in his retrospective coverage. He also wanted the Jackson 5 represented because, regardless of how one reads their cultural function, they invented a vocabulary later genres ran with. He was lukewarm on *Music of My Mind* (rougher than Stevie's run that followed) and instead pushed *Songs in the Key of Life* harder.

Rounds 1 and 2 locked in the seven shared picks. Rounds 3 and 4 hashed out the Temptations question (Scaruffi got *Cloud Nine* in over Fantano's comp). Round 5 settled on *Music of My Mind* for the auteur-origin slot. Round 6 was the painful one: who closes the list. We coin-flipped *Diana Ross & The Supremes Greatest Hits* against *The Ultimate Collection: Smokey Robinson & The Miracles* for the singles-canon honor; the Supremes won. Then Marvin's *Here, My Dear* vs the Jackson 5's *ABC* for the final auteur-vs-pop seat; *Here, My Dear* won.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Marvin Gaye — *What's Going On* (1971)

The album that ended the argument over whether a Motown artist could be an album artist. Gordy reportedly hated it, refused to release it, and was forced into it by Marvin's refusal to record anything else. What landed was a suite — songs flowing into each other, lyrics indexed to Vietnam, urban poverty, ecology, and the spiritual disorientation of a country at war with itself — anchored by a vocal performance that layered Marvin against Marvin against Marvin until the lead line was barely distinguishable from the harmony. The Funk Brothers' rhythm section is at its loosest and most jazz-inflected here; the strings are arranged with a restraint that the singles era rarely permitted.

Scaruffi treats this as Motown's only true masterpiece by his criteria — a unified artistic statement rather than a vehicle for singles. Fantano has spoken about it in retrospective contexts as a foundational soul-album text, the record that legitimized the format for the entire R&B lineage that follows. Neither critic argued for anything else at #1. The "What's Going On" / "What's Happening Brother" / "Flyin' High (In the Friendly Sky)" / "Save the Children" opening run is one of the great sequenced stretches of any soul LP. The closing "Inner City Blues (Make Me Wanna Holler)" is the political bookend the album was building toward. Everything Motown did after 1971 — and a great deal of Black popular music in general — operates downstream of this record.

##### 2. Stevie Wonder — *Songs in the Key of Life* (1976)

The maximalist statement of Stevie's classic run: a double LP with a bonus EP, the kind of record that exists because the artist had earned total creative control and intended to use it. Scope is the point — gospel ("Have a Talk with God"), funk ("I Wish," "Sir Duke"), Latin ("Another Star," "Ngiculela – Es Una Historia – I Am Singing"), ballad ("Isn't She Lovely," "If It's Magic"), social commentary ("Village Ghetto Land," "Black Man"), and the long-form jazz-fusion meditation ("As") that operates as the album's emotional climax.
