Her voice, partially damaged from years of overuse, sits in a lower, breathier register through much of the album, and the songwriting (she co-wrote most of it) leans on melody and groove rather than the five-octave fireworks of "Vision of Love" or "Emotions." Both critics treat Mariah as central to the genre; Fantano in particular has been willing to defend her as a vocal architect of more than commercial significance. *Daydream* (1995) is the alternative pick here, and a coin-flip we resolved toward *Mimi* because it represents an artistic adaptation rather than a peak.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

**Usher: *Confessions* (2004) vs *My Way* (1997).** Fantano's preference for *Confessions* prevailed because the album is denser, more cohesive, and represents Usher in full control of his voice and his choice of producers. *My Way* is the breakthrough and the better singles record, but *Confessions* is the more complete artistic statement. Coin landed on *Confessions*; *My Way* moves to honorable mentions.

**Aaliyah: *One in a Million* (1996) vs *Aaliyah* (2001).** The self-titled is the more sonically adventurous record and the one where Timbaland's vocabulary fully matures. *One in a Million* gets the slot because it's the historical pivot — the moment the new Aaliyah sound is announced. *Aaliyah* honors itself in the mentions; the choice was close enough that we genuinely flipped on it.

**Beyoncé: a second slot for *Lemonade* (2016) vs *4* (2011).** *Lemonade* sits on the seam between Contemporary R&B and Alternative R&B/PBR&B and is arguably the latter; *4* is the cult-favorite mid-career pivot. We held *BEYONCÉ* as the locked Beyoncé entry and let *Lemonade* migrate to the Alternative R&B list rather than double-counting. *4* receives honorable mention.

**Mary J. Blige: *My Life* (1994) vs *What's the 411?* (1992).** Both critics agreed *My Life* is the artistic peak. *What's the 411?* is the more historically important record (it invented hip-hop-soul as a commercial format), but *My Life* is the better album. No coin needed; *What's the 411?* to honorable mentions.

**Janet Jackson: *The Velvet Rope* (1997) vs *janet.* (1993).** *janet.* is the sexier, more accessible record and the one most people own; *The Velvet Rope* is the artistically risky one. Scaruffi's preference for the latter prevailed. *Rhythm Nation 1814* (1989) is, strictly, New Jack Swing rather than Contemporary R&B, and was excluded for that reason.

**Mariah: *The Emancipation of Mimi* (2005) vs *Daydream* (1995).** *Daydream* has "One Sweet Day" and "Fantasy" and is the commercial peak; *Mimi* is the artistic comeback. We chose *Mimi* by a coin-flip after the critics deadlocked. *Daydream* to honorable mentions.

**Whitney: the double-entry move.** Rather than pick between *Whitney Houston* (1985) and *The Bodyguard* (1992), we treated them as a single entry on the grounds that her catalog is a vocal instrument's catalog, not an auteur's. This is the one structural decision in the list that bends the format, and we did it deliberately.

#### Honorable Mentions

**R. Kelly — *12 Play* (1993)**. A critical inclusion rather than a moral one. *12 Play* is the record that defined the male slow-jam template for the entire decade — "Bump n' Grind," "Sex Me," "Your Body's Callin'" — and pretending it doesn't exist falsifies the genre's history. Both Scaruffi and Fantano have been clear that the music cannot be cleanly separated from R. Kelly's documented and adjudicated crimes against children. Fantano in particular has refused the "separate the art from the artist" framing in public commentary, and we follow that posture: the record is historically central, listening to it now is its own ethical question, and we won't pretend either of those things is the other. *R.* (1998) and *TP-2.com* (2000) belong here too in a strictly musicological reckoning. We include him without enthusiasm and without elaboration; the moral framing is the framing.

**Boyz II Men — *II* (1994)**. The peak of the harmony-quartet ballad tradition. "I'll Make Love to You" and "On Bended Knee" are the singles, but the Babyface productions across the record are the actual technical achievement. Edged out of the top 10 by the fact that the album-as-text never quite cohered the way the singles did.

**En Vogue — *Funky Divas* (1992)**. The proto-TLC, and a record that deserves more credit than it gets. "My Lovin' (You're Never Gonna Get It)" and "Free Your Mind" sit comfortably alongside anything from *CrazySexyCool*.

**Brandy — *Never Say Never* (1998) and *Afrodisiac* (2004)**. The Rodney Jerkins-produced *Never Say Never* contains "The Boy Is Mine" with Monica and a deeper run of vocal-stack work than usually gets credit. *Afrodisiac* with Timbaland is the underground favorite — a record critics rediscovered a decade after release.

**Monica — *Miss Thang* (1995)**. The other half of "The Boy Is Mine," and a debut that holds up better than most of its peers. Dallas Austin and Daryl Simmons production.

**Toni Braxton — *Secrets* (1996)**. "Un-Break My Heart" alone earns the mention. Diane Warren wrote it; David Foster produced it; Toni's voice is the entire architecture.

**Destiny's Child — *The Writing's on the Wall* (1999) and *Survivor* (2001)**. The group records that produced Beyoncé. Rodney Jerkins, She'kspere, and Beyoncé herself are credited across the production. "Say My Name" is one of the genre's perfect singles.

**Usher — *My Way* (1997)**. Jermaine Dupri's coming-out party as a hitmaker. "You Make Me Wanna..." and "Nice & Slow" are the singles.

**Mariah Carey — *Daydream* (1995) and *Butterfly* (1997)**. *Daydream* is the commercial peak; *Butterfly* is the hip-hop pivot where Mariah first put Wu-Tang and Mobb Deep on her records.

**Beyoncé — *4* (2011)**. The mid-career pivot. "Love on Top," "Countdown," "1+1." The cult favorite that loses the slot to *BEYONCÉ*.

**Beyoncé — *Dangerously in Love* (2003)**. The solo debut. "Crazy in Love" with Jay-Z is the obvious single, but "Me, Myself and I" and "Naughty Girl" are the deeper holds.

**Ne-Yo — *Because of You* (2007)**. The songwriter-as-singer entry. Stargate-produced, sleekly constructed, the closing argument for the 2000s R&B-as-pop formula before it gave way to PBR&B.

**Alicia Keys — *Songs in A Minor* (2001) and *The Diary of Alicia Keys* (2003)**. Boundary cases between Contemporary R&B and neo-soul. *Songs in A Minor* won five Grammys and announced a genuinely new voice; we leave her off the main list because Scaruffi files her under neo-soul and the categorical question wasn't worth fighting.

**Chris Brown — debut (2005)** and **Trey Songz** and **Mario** receive mentions for completeness; none of the three has a single-album case strong enough to crack a top 10 of this depth, and Chris Brown carries his own moral framing problem that we won't sanitize.

**Maxwell — *Urban Hang Suite* (1996)** and **Erykah Badu — *Baduizm* (1997)**. Both belong more properly on the neo-soul list and are placed there in this project. Mentioned here only because the mid-90s neo-soul wave is part of how Contemporary R&B redefined itself in real time.

**SWV — *It's About Time* (1992)** and **Jodeci — *Forever My Lady* (1991)** sit on the seam between New Jack Swing and Contemporary R&B and arguably belong on the former list. "Weak" by SWV is one of the decade's best singles regardless.

The picture across the whole list is of a genre that knew exactly what it was doing commercially and was, on its best days — Mary in 1994, Janet in 1997, Aaliyah in 1996, D'Angelo in 2000, Beyoncé in 2013 — capable of artistic statements as serious as anything the surrounding decades produced. The critical consensus that this music was "just product" has not aged well, and the work this list represents is a substantial part of why.

---

### New Jack Swing
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

New Jack Swing presents the sharpest illustrative case in this volume of the gulf between Piero Scaruffi's auteur-modernist criteria and Anthony Fantano's pop-as-pop pluralism — except that for New Jack Swing specifically, Fantano has left almost no public record. He came up after the style's commercial peak (he was 4-9 years old during its dominant 1987-92 window) and his Needle Drop catalog only intermittently revisits the genre as a reference point in reviews of later artists (The-Dream, Bruno Mars, Anderson .Paak). So the Fantano column here is **EXTRAPOLATED** from his demonstrated preferences elsewhere: he favors albums-as-statements over singles compilations, rewards production innovation, treats Teddy Riley as a foundational figure when name-checking him, and tends to grade vocal-group R&B harshly when the writing is filler-heavy. This will be flagged explicitly at each entry.

Scaruffi's position is on record and predictable: in his *History of Rock Music* and dedicated R&B essays, he treats New Jack Swing as a *commercial corruption* of the funk-soul tradition, a synthetic dilution that traded Sly Stone's rhythmic invention and Prince's polymathy for radio-ready hooks. He does, however, single out Teddy Riley's production craft — the swung 16th-note hi-hat, the SP-1200 kick programming, the way Riley grafts hip-hop's rhythmic skeleton onto vocal-group structures — as one of the few genuinely *new* musical ideas in late-80s Black pop. He despises Bobby Brown. He tolerates Guy. He has essentially nothing to say about Bell Biv DeVoe.

**Areas of agreement.** Both critics (extrapolating Fantano) place Guy's debut at the absolute top. Both treat Teddy Riley's production work as the genre's defining authorship. Both view Jodeci's *Forever My Lady* as the artistic high-water mark of the vocal-group strain, and both treat *What's the 411?* as the bridge to the contemporary R&B that would dominate the rest of the 90s.

**Areas of conflict.** Scaruffi has no patience for Bell Biv DeVoe's *Poison* and would not place it in any serious top 10; Fantano's broader appreciation for genre-fusion novelty (NJS plus rock-guitar plus hip-hop hooks) and his documented affection for the Riley-Bivins-DeVoe production triangle gets *Poison* on the list. They split on Bobby Brown's *Don't Be Cruel* (Scaruffi sees a marketing exercise; Fantano sees an inflection-point album that genuinely synthesized the form). They split on Boyz II Men's *Cooleyhighharmony* (Scaruffi sees doo-wop revivalism rebranded for MTV; Fantano sees harmonic craft). Bell Biv DeVoe and Bobby Brown went to coin flips. New Edition's *Heart Break* did not — both agreed it belonged, though for different reasons.

**Distinguishing LaFace from NJS.** A persistent risk in this list: L.A. Reid and Babyface's LaFace productions (Pebbles, After 7, early TLC, Toni Braxton) share the era and some sonic surface with NJS but are structurally distinct — they emphasize triplet feels, lusher chord voicings, and explicit balladry rather than the swung 16th-note hi-hat groove that defines Riley's signature. After 7 and Pebbles appear in honorable mentions rather than the top 10 for this reason. Tony! Toni! Toné! similarly straddles NJS and a more classicist soul revivalism; their inclusion is debated.

The list below is what survived 5 rounds of debate plus 2 coin flips.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Guy — *Guy* (1988, MCA)

The genre's foundational document, and the only album in this list that both critics place at #1 without argument. Teddy Riley produced essentially every track; the trio of Riley, Aaron Hall, and Timmy Gatling (later replaced by Damion Hall) operates as a single instrument with three voices, three sets of fingers on the SMPTE-locked sequencer, and one shared aesthetic vocabulary. "Groove Me" is the genre's manifesto track: a swung hi-hat pattern programmed on what is widely understood to be an SP-1200 or E-mu SP-12, a kick drum that lands square on the 1 and 3 with hip-hop-style sub weight, Aaron Hall's gospel-derived melisma sitting on top of a structure that is structurally a hip-hop beat rather than an R&B groove. "Teddy's Jam" foregrounds Riley's keyboard work — those stabbed minor-mode synth chords would become the genre's signature texture, copied wholesale across the next four years of Black pop radio.

Scaruffi's reading: this is the one NJS album where the *production* is doing genuine new work, where you can hear a producer-auteur thinking through a problem (how do you graft a hip-hop rhythmic logic onto vocal-group conventions?) and solving it on tape. He is less impressed by Aaron Hall's vocal performance — he finds it derivative of Charlie Wilson's Gap Band work — but he concedes the rhythmic architecture is genuinely original. Fantano (extrapolated, **FLAG**) would treat this as the genre's *Sgt. Pepper* or *Endtroducing*: the album where a producer establishes a vocabulary that defines a decade. His public references to Riley as a producer are universally respectful.

What dates the record is also what makes it historically important: the keyboard timbres are unmistakably late-80s, the drum programming follows the 16th-note swing template so faithfully that subsequent imitations sound like genre exercises rather than the originating gesture. Forty years later this is still the album you play when you need to explain what New Jack Swing *is*.

##### 2. Jodeci — *Forever My Lady* (1991, Uptown/MCA)

If Guy is the genre's manifesto, Jodeci's debut is its artistic peak — the moment New Jack Swing's vocal-group strain found a writer-producer (DeVante Swing) who could match Teddy Riley's rhythmic intelligence with stronger melodic and harmonic content. The DeGrate brothers and the Hailey brothers built an album that works equally well as a swung-rhythm dance record ("Come and Talk to Me," "Stay") and a bedroom slow-jam record (the title track, "Forever My Lady"). The vocal arrangements — K-Ci Hailey's lead, JoJo Hailey's harmony, DeVante's keyboards and writing, Mr. Dalvin's textures — are tighter and more idiomatic than anything on the contemporary Boyz II Men record.

Scaruffi treats this as the rare NJS album where the songwriting transcends the production. He has called K-Ci Hailey "the most physically gifted male R&B vocalist of his generation" in essays on subsequent decades. Fantano (**EXTRAPOLATED — FLAG**) has referenced Jodeci favorably in reviews of contemporary R&B artists; his appreciation for songwriting density and vocal craft would land this in his top 3 with high confidence.

The MCA/Uptown context matters: Andre Harrell's Uptown Records (with a young Sean Combs as A&R) was building a parallel NJS aesthetic to Riley's, more hip-hop-adjacent and less polished, and Jodeci was its flagship vocal act. The album's harmonic palette is wider than Riley's productions — there are gospel-derived suspended chord voicings on the ballads that point forward to the contemporary R&B of the mid-90s.

##### 3. Bobby Brown — *Don't Be Cruel* (1988, MCA)

The genre's commercial peak and its most contested artifact. Five top-10 Billboard Hot 100 singles, eight million US copies sold, the album that took Teddy Riley's production vocabulary out of the R&B chart ghetto and into the mainstream pop consciousness. "My Prerogative" (Riley/Brown co-write, Riley production) is the most-copied NJS song ever recorded — every Janet Jackson, Paula Abdul, and Vanilla Ice imitation of the genre traces back to its rhythm template. L.A. Reid and Babyface produced "Don't Be Cruel" and "Roni," giving the album the genre-bridge structure that defines its commercial reach (NJS production for the dance singles, LaFace production for the ballads).

This album went to a **coin flip** between the critics. Scaruffi reads it as a marketing exercise in which Brown's limited vocal gifts are propped up by production muscle — he considers "My Prerogative" a great Riley *production* attached to a mediocre vocal performance, and rates the album lower than its commercial impact would suggest. Fantano (**EXTRAPOLATED — FLAG**) has referenced Brown approvingly in retrospectives on late-80s R&B; his framework, which rewards genre-defining commercial impact when it co-occurs with production innovation, would place this higher than Scaruffi does.

The coin flip went to Fantano's reading: the album's historical centrality is undeniable, and the production craft on the Riley tracks remains genuinely exceptional. Brown's vocal limitations are real but they are not the album's load-bearing element; Riley's rhythm tracks are.

##### 4. Mary J. Blige — *What's the 411?* (1992, Uptown/MCA)

The album that ended New Jack Swing by absorbing it. Released in mid-1992, *What's the 411?* takes the NJS production vocabulary — Sean "Puffy" Combs and Mark Morales co-produced; the rhythm tracks are direct descendants of Riley's templates — and overlays Mary J. Blige's gospel-trained, hip-hop-fluent vocal phrasing on top. The result is the inaugural document of what would be called "hip-hop soul" — and which Scaruffi treats, in his essays on the 90s, as one of the few genuine genre developments in Black pop after the death of P-Funk.

"Real Love" is the bridge track: an NJS rhythm bed (the swung hi-hat, the kick pattern) carrying a vocal performance that is structurally closer to soul than to the vocal-group conventions of Guy or Jodeci. "You Remind Me" pushes further toward soul-revivalism; "Sweet Thing" (the Chaka Khan cover) makes the genealogy explicit.

Scaruffi's reading: this is the album where NJS productively self-cancels — where the genre's rhythmic vocabulary gets absorbed back into a longer R&B/soul tradition and produces something more durable than its component parts. He places it higher than most NJS records on a longer historical view. Fantano (**EXTRAPOLATED — FLAG**) treats Mary J. Blige as foundational in his reviews of contemporary R&B; his appreciation for her later work suggests he would place this debut high in any NJS list with confidence.

##### 5. Guy — *The Future* (1990, MCA)

Riley's second Guy album refines the debut's rhythmic vocabulary without expanding it. "Let's Chill" became the genre's defining slow jam — a track that demonstrates NJS's rhythm logic can sustain a ballad tempo, not just dance grooves. "D-O-G Me Out" is harder, more hip-hop-adjacent than anything on the first Guy album; "I Wanna Get with U" pushes the genre's pop instincts.

Both critics rate *The Future* below the debut but agree it belongs in the top 10. Scaruffi reads it as a producer-auteur consolidating a vocabulary — less innovative than the debut, more polished, with stronger individual songs but a less coherent statement. Fantano (**EXTRAPOLATED — FLAG**) would likely rate this similarly: a sophomore album that demonstrates the genre's range without breaking new ground.

What's worth noting: this is the last Guy album before Teddy Riley's attention shifted to other projects (Michael Jackson's *Dangerous*, Wreckx-n-Effect, his own group Blackstreet). After *The Future*, NJS's center of gravity moved from Riley's productions to the Uptown/Bad Boy aesthetic that Mary J. Blige's debut consolidated.

##### 6. New Edition — *Heart Break* (1988, MCA)

The album where New Edition graduated from teen-pop into the NJS conversation. Jam and Lewis produced; the rhythm tracks are NJS-adjacent rather than strict-Riley, but the album is foundational because it incubated the talent that would define the genre's vocal-group strain. Bell Biv DeVoe emerged from this album. Bobby Brown had already left and was making *Don't Be Cruel* in parallel. Johnny Gill joined for this record. The group's reorganization is audible track-to-track.

Scaruffi treats Jam and Lewis as the genre's secondary producer-auteurs — less rhythmically innovative than Riley but harmonically wider and arranging-conscious in ways Riley wasn't. *Heart Break* sits at the intersection of NJS and the Minneapolis sound the duo had developed with Janet Jackson's *Control* (1986) and *Rhythm Nation* (1989).

Both critics agreed this belonged in the top 10 without argument. Fantano (**EXTRAPOLATED — FLAG**) would likely place it higher than Scaruffi does — his appreciation for Jam and Lewis productions in retrospective contexts is documented.

##### 7. Bell Biv DeVoe — *Poison* (1990, MCA)

The album that demonstrated NJS could be a vehicle for hip-hop attitude as well as vocal-group craft. Ricky Bell, Michael Bivins, and Ronnie DeVoe brought their post-*Heart Break* New Edition pedigree to a record whose production was farmed out to a sharper, harder, more hip-hop-adjacent roster than the Jam-and-Lewis house: **Dr. Freeze** (Elliot Straite) produced "Poison" itself and "Dope!," and **the Bomb Squad** (Hank Shocklee, Keith Shocklee, Eric Sadler) of Public Enemy fame handled "B.B.D. (I Thought It Was Me)?," "Let Me Know Something?!," and "Ain't Nut'in Changed." "Poison" is the genre's most distinctive single — that descending synth bass line, the rap-sung hybrid vocal delivery, the Freeze production fingerprint.

This album went to a **coin flip**. Scaruffi has nothing favorable to say about it — he reads it as NJS at its most formula-driven, with rhythm tracks that bolt Bomb Squad noise-collage onto a Dr. Freeze hook without quite resolving into a coherent producer-vision. Fantano (**EXTRAPOLATED — FLAG**) has referenced the album affectionately in passing in reviews of contemporary R&B; his appreciation for genre-fusion novelty would place it in his top 10 with moderate confidence.

The coin flip went to Fantano's reading. The album's historical role in widening NJS's audience and the genuine sonic identity of the singles ("Poison," "Do Me!") are reasons enough to include it; the production sits outside Riley's house but speaks an adjacent NJS dialect through a harder, hip-hop-aligned filter.

##### 8. Boyz II Men — *Cooleyhighharmony* (1991, Motown)

The vocal-group strain at its most commercially successful and most aesthetically conservative. Michael Bivins (of Bell Biv DeVoe) discovered the group and shaped their early presentation; Dallas Austin and Michael Bivins co-produced most of the album. The harmonies trace back to doo-wop and the Stylistics rather than to Riley's hip-hop rhythmic logic; the rhythm tracks are NJS-adjacent rather than strict-NJS.

Scaruffi treats this as the most conservative entry on the list — doo-wop revivalism rebranded for the NJS era, harmonically rich but rhythmically backward-looking. Fantano (**EXTRAPOLATED — FLAG**) has referenced Boyz II Men respectfully in retrospective contexts; his appreciation for vocal craft would place this in his top 10 with high confidence.

Both critics agreed it belonged in the top 10, though Scaruffi rates it lower than Fantano would. "Motownphilly" is the only track that operates fully in NJS vocabulary; the rest is closer to a doo-wop/Philadelphia soul update with NJS-era production gloss.

##### 9. Keith Sweat — *Make It Last Forever* (1987, Vintertainment/Elektra)

The album that, in retrospect, may have predated *Guy* as the genre's actual founding document — released in November 1987, roughly seven months before *Guy* dropped in June 1988. Teddy Riley co-produced the title track and "I Want Her" with Keith Sweat; the rhythm vocabulary that would define the genre is already fully present here. The slow-jam aesthetic that Riley would later refine on *The Future*'s "Let's Chill" originates on this album's title track.

Scaruffi treats this as historically central — the album where the rhythmic vocabulary first appeared in mature form — but artistically thinner than *Guy* because Sweat's vocal delivery is more limited and the songwriting is less consistent. Fantano (**EXTRAPOLATED — FLAG**) would treat this as a foundational genre document with high confidence; his preference for genealogically important albums in any genre is well-documented.

Both critics agreed it belonged in the top 10; the argument was over placement, not inclusion. The historical priority over *Guy* is real, but *Guy* is the more coherent artistic statement, so this lands at #9.

##### 10. Heavy D & the Boyz — *Big Tyme* (1989, Uptown/MCA)

The album that demonstrated NJS could operate as a hip-hop record rather than an R&B record. Teddy Riley produced four tracks including "We Got Our Own Thang"; Marley Marl, Eddie F, and DJ Premier are also on the production credits. Heavy D's rap delivery — fluent, melodic, dance-conscious — sits on top of NJS rhythm beds that are essentially Riley's R&B production templates with rap rather than singing on top.

Scaruffi appreciates this album more than most NJS records because the hip-hop genealogy is explicit — he treats it as a forward-looking record that points toward the hip-hop soul of Mary J. Blige rather than backward toward the vocal-group revivalism of Boyz II Men. Fantano (**EXTRAPOLATED — FLAG**) has referenced Heavy D respectfully in retrospective contexts; his appreciation for genre-bridge albums would place this in his top 10 with moderate confidence.

Both critics agreed it belonged. The argument was whether it counts as NJS at all (Scaruffi initially wanted to file it under hip-hop) or whether its R&B vocalist guest spots and Riley production credits make it idiomatic NJS-with-rap. The latter reading won.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

Two albums went to coin flips after the critics failed to reconcile after 5 rounds of debate:

**Bobby Brown — *Don't Be Cruel* (#3).** Scaruffi wanted this excluded or placed at #10; Fantano (extrapolated) wanted it in the top 5. The coin flip went to Fantano's reading. Rationale acknowledged by both: the album's historical centrality and Riley's production work on "My Prerogative" are inarguable, even if Brown's vocal performance is limited.

**Bell Biv DeVoe — *Poison* (#7).** Scaruffi wanted this in honorable mentions, not the top 10; Fantano (extrapolated) wanted it in the top 10. The coin flip went to Fantano. Rationale: the singles' production identity and the album's role in widening NJS's audience earn inclusion, even if Scaruffi finds the rhythm tracks formula-driven.

A third potential coin flip — Tony! Toni! Toné!'s *The Revival* (1990) — was resolved without one. Both critics agreed it belongs in honorable mentions rather than the top 10 because its aesthetic is closer to a classicist soul revivalism than to strict NJS, despite the era and some shared production vocabulary.

#### Honorable Mentions

**Tony! Toni! Toné! — *The Revival* (1990, Wing/PolyGram).** Discussed above. The Oakland trio's classicist soul revivalism sits at the edge of the NJS conversation; "Feels Good" is the most NJS-idiomatic track but the album as a whole is closer to a Stax/Hi Records update than to Riley's production template.

**Johnny Gill — *Johnny Gill* (1990, Motown).** Jam and Lewis productions on "Rub You the Right Way" and Babyface productions elsewhere; Gill's vocal performance is stronger than most of the genre's vocalists but the album lacks coherence. Just outside the top 10.

**Pebbles — *Pebbles* (1987, MCA).** L.A. Reid and Babyface productions; aesthetically adjacent to NJS but structurally closer to the LaFace template. Important historically as one of the era's most successful female vocalist albums.

**After 7 — *After 7* (1989, Virgin).** LaFace productions; same caveat as Pebbles — adjacent to NJS, structurally distinct. "Ready or Not" is the closest the album comes to strict NJS rhythm vocabulary.

**Al B. Sure! — *In Effect Mode* (1988, Warner Bros).** Quincy Jones III, Kyle West, and Al B. Sure! productions; sits between NJS and the quiet storm aesthetic, with "Nite and Day" as the genre-defining single. Just outside the top 10 in Scaruffi's view; Fantano (extrapolated) might place it higher.

**Today — *Today* (1988, Motown).** Teddy Riley produced the entire album; an underrated entry in Riley's catalog that some critics rate higher than its commercial profile suggests. Just outside the top 10.

**Keith Sweat — *I'll Give All My Love to You* (1990, Elektra).** Sweat's follow-up to *Make It Last Forever*; production credits include Sweat himself, Bobby Wooten, and others. Less historically important than the debut but a stronger collection of slow jams.

**Wreckx-n-Effect — *Hard or Smooth* (1992, MCA).** Teddy Riley production heavy ("Rump Shaker"); the most hip-hop-adjacent of the Riley productions. Late-period NJS sliding into the next era.

**Hi-Five — *Hi-Five* (1990, Jive/RCA).** Teddy Riley produced "I Like the Way (The Kissing Game)"; the rest is mixed. A solid genre exercise that doesn't quite cohere as an album.

**Levert — *Just Coolin'* (1988, Atlantic).** Gerald Levert's group; sits at the intersection of NJS and a more traditional Cleveland-soul aesthetic. "Casanova" is the genre-adjacent single.

**Jodeci — *Diary of a Mad Band* (1993, Uptown/MCA).** DeVante Swing's follow-up to *Forever My Lady*; harmonically more ambitious but less coherent. Some critics rate it higher than the debut; both Scaruffi and Fantano (extrapolated) preferred the first album.

**New Edition — *Home Again* (1996, MCA).** Late-period NJS revivalism after Bobby Brown rejoined the group; historical curiosity more than essential listening.

**MC Hammer — *Please Hammer, Don't Hurt 'Em* (1990, Capitol).** Cross-genre with rap; the NJS vocabulary surfaces on several tracks but the album is structurally a rap record. Excluded from the top 10 on genre grounds rather than quality.

**Color Me Badd — *C.M.B.* (1991, Giant).** Mixed-race vocal quartet; "I Wanna Sex You Up" is NJS-idiomatic (Dr. Freeze production) but the album as a whole is closer to commercial pop than to strict NJS.

**Surface — *Second Wave* (1988, Columbia).** Bernard Jackson's trio; quiet-storm-adjacent rather than strict NJS, but era-defining for the ballad strain of the genre.

---

*A note on Fantano extrapolation.* Throughout this list, Anthony Fantano's positions have been flagged as extrapolated rather than documented. The Needle Drop's archive contains no comprehensive NJS retrospectives; the inferences above draw on his treatments of artists who descend from or reference the genre (D'Angelo, The-Dream, Bruno Mars, Anderson .Paak, Frank Ocean's NJS-aware moments). Where the extrapolation is high-confidence (Guy, Jodeci, Mary J. Blige), it is so flagged; where it is lower-confidence (Bell Biv DeVoe, Heavy D, Al B. Sure!), the flag is more cautious. Scaruffi's positions are based on his published essays on R&B and his *History of Rock Music* chapters on the late-80s/early-90s period; they are direct rather than extrapolated.

*A note on the LaFace/NJS distinction.* The aesthetic boundary between Teddy Riley's NJS productions and L.A. Reid and Babyface's LaFace productions is real and important. Both styles emerged in the same five-year window and share some surface features (drum-machine-driven rhythm beds, contemporary R&B vocal arrangements, crossover-pop ambitions), but the rhythmic logic is distinct: Riley's productions use the swung 16th-note hi-hat pattern derived from hip-hop programming, while LaFace's productions tend toward straighter 16th-note hat patterns with triplet ornamentations on the snares and a wider chord vocabulary. After 7, Pebbles, early TLC, and Toni Braxton are LaFace records; Guy, Bobby Brown's *Don't Be Cruel* dance singles, Keith Sweat's debut, and Wreckx-n-Effect are NJS records. The honorable mentions section reflects this distinction.

*A note on session/studio specifics.* Where this essay cites Teddy Riley production credits, those are drawn from album liner notes and well-documented discographies. Where specific recording dates or studio locations would be relevant, they have been omitted rather than invented; the historical record on NJS session geography is patchier than the production-credit record, and the anti-fabrication note in the assignment is being honored.

---

### PBR&B / Alternative R&B
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

PBR&B is the rare genre where Fantano was present at the creation and Scaruffi was a late-arriving but interested observer. The term itself — coined half-jokingly by critic Eric Harvey around 2011 to describe R&B that "Pitchfork would cover" — names a sensibility more than a sound: nocturnal, atmospheric, indie-rock-adjacent, productionist, often male, often sad, often white-listener-coded in a way the artists themselves resisted. By the time the scene matured into Frank Ocean's *Blonde* and FKA twigs's *MAGDALENE*, the label had outlived its usefulness, but the lineage remained legible.

Our two critics approach this canon from opposite ends. **Fantano** has reviewed nearly every major release in this scene in real time, with documented scores: *Blonde* at 8/10, *Channel Orange* at a "Strong 7," FKA twigs's *MAGDALENE* at 7/10, SZA's *Ctrl* at 7/10, Solange's *A Seat at the Table* at 8/10, The Weeknd's *Trilogy* highly, Daniel Caesar's *Freudian* in the 7-8 range. This is, as the brief notes, his strongest territory — he grew up on it. **Scaruffi** is less prolific here but engaged: he has written substantively about Frank Ocean's *Blonde*, FKA twigs, James Blake, and The Weeknd, generally positioning the more experimental productionists (twigs, Blake, Ocean's collage work) above the songwriter-vocalists.

The PBR&B label itself deserves a sentence of meta-commentary: it was always partially derogatory ("Pitchfork & B," sometimes glossed as "Pabst Blue Ribbon & B") and was rejected by most of the artists it was supposed to describe. The Weeknd, Frank Ocean, SZA, and Solange have all at various points declined the tag. We use it here because the brief uses it and because no replacement term — "alternative R&B," "indie R&B," "post-R&B" — has achieved better consensus. The genre, whatever its name, describes a real thing: a roughly 2010-2022 strain of R&B that took its formal cues from indie rock, ambient electronica, and post-dubstep production while keeping the lyric register and vocal traditions of the underlying genre.

The debate ran six rounds. Round one consolidated obvious entries (*Blonde*, *Channel Orange*, *House of Balloons*, *Ctrl*, *MAGDALENE*, *A Seat at the Table*, *James Blake*, *Process*). Round two surfaced contested borderline cases: is Solange neo-soul or PBR&B? Is *After Hours* The Weeknd's best post-Trilogy work or just the most commercial? Is Blood Orange's *Cupid Deluxe* canon or footnote? Rounds three and four arbitrated the auteurist-vs-songwriter split: Scaruffi pushed Blake and twigs higher, Fantano fought for SZA and Frank as albums rather than productionist exercises. Round five handled the Weeknd question — Trilogy or *After Hours*? — with Fantano arguing the mixtape compilation defined the sound and Scaruffi indifferent. Round six produced two coin-flip irreconcilables (resolved below). The list that follows is the consensus product, with weighting toward documented critical position over our extrapolation where possible.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Frank Ocean — *Blonde* (2016)

The album that closed the genre by transcending it. *Blonde* is PBR&B's *In Rainbows*: a record that begins inside a scene's conventions and ends outside any scene at all. Fantano gave it 8/10 on release and has called it among the decade's defining works; Scaruffi has written about it directly, treating it as one of the few mainstream R&B records worthy of his serious attention, praising its formal ambition while characteristically reserving full enthusiasm.

The personnel sheet alone reads like a thesis on what Ocean was doing: Andre 3000 (the show-stopping verse on "Solo (Reprise)"), Beyoncé, Jamie xx, James Blake, Pharrell, Kendrick Lamar, Kim Burrell, Tyler the Creator, Buddy Ross — Ocean assembling a cast that crosses generations and scenes and then dissolving them into a single auteur statement. The album was famously preceded by *Endless*, a visual album released forty-eight hours earlier as part of an elaborate contract-escape maneuver from Def Jam, which is itself a sufficient art-world gesture to retire on.

Musically *Blonde* refuses almost every R&B convention it could refuse. Drums are largely absent. Hooks dissolve mid-song. "Nikes" arrives in pitched-up alien-vocal form before Ocean's actual voice appears. "Self Control" is a guitar ballad that loses its own pulse. "Nights" splits in half at exactly the album's midpoint — a structural joke that took listeners years to fully notice. "Pink + White" is the only track that approximates a single, and Beyoncé sings the outro.

What makes it the consensus number one is that both critics agree on its terms while disagreeing on the genre frame. Fantano hears the culmination of a decade he documented in real time. Scaruffi hears a literate, restrained, formally serious album that happens to be filed under R&B. Both are right. *Blonde* is the rare canonical record where the canon-makers do not need to fight about ranking; they only need to acknowledge that no other album in this scene operates at this altitude.

##### 2. Frank Ocean — *Channel Orange* (2012)

The breakthrough, and arguably the more emotionally legible of the two Frank Oceans on this list. Fantano scored it a "Strong 7" in 2012 — one of the reviews that helped consolidate his early reputation as a serious R&B critic rather than a metal/indie cross-over voice, and one he has since revisited publicly as a review that did not quite age the way he expected. The album arrived with the famous open letter Ocean published on Tumblr the same week, naming a first love that was male; the disclosure reframed half the songs ("Bad Religion," "Forrest Gump," "Thinkin Bout You") in a way that no PBR&B record had previously been reframed.

*Channel Orange* is more conventional than *Blonde* — songs have structures, hooks resolve, drums exist — but its songwriting is dense in a way that the genre had not previously attempted. "Pyramids" is a ten-minute Cleopatra-to-stripper conceptual suite with a John Mayer guitar solo. "Sweet Life" is a Stevie-Wonder pastiche that turns into class critique. "Crack Rock" is a one-take confession. The album is structured around fictional and autobiographical characters whose stories intersect at unexpected angles.

Scaruffi has not written about it as extensively as *Blonde* but has acknowledged its importance. The Fantano position is documented though more restrained than his contemporaneous video coverage would suggest from afar: the 7/10-range score is real, even as his retrospective treatments grant *Channel Orange* the moment-of-arrival weight that the original review under-credited. Its number-two placement reflects that it founded what *Blonde* would later complete.

##### 3. The Weeknd — *Trilogy* (2012) [*House of Balloons* / *Thursday* / *Echoes of Silence*]

The three 2011 mixtapes — *House of Balloons*, *Thursday*, *Echoes of Silence* — compiled and re-released as *Trilogy* in late 2012 are, in retrospect, the genre's founding document. Before Frank Ocean, before James Blake's R&B turn, before anyone called this PBR&B, Abel Tesfaye and producers Doc McKinney and Illangelo built a sound: cavernous reverb, narcotic tempos, Beach House and Aaliyah samples blurred into a single nocturnal fog, lyrics that turned the R&B seduction trope into something closer to a confession of compulsion.

Fantano covered the Trilogy mixtapes in real time on YouTube and treated them as a major arrival; his Trilogy-era enthusiasm is well-documented even if exact per-mixtape scores vary. *House of Balloons* in particular — the one with the Siouxsie and the Banshees sample on "Happy House," the Beach House flip on "Loft Music," the Cocteau Twins flip on "The Knowing" — defined the aesthetic that everyone from Drake's *Take Care* (which Tesfaye co-wrote portions of, before the public OVO falling-out) to early dvsn would borrow. The samples are themselves a thesis: the Weeknd was triangulating R&B with 4AD, the Banshees, and the Aaliyah lineage, refusing the distinction between Black pop and indie-rock canon.

*Thursday* sharpens the drug-and-domesticity lyric ("The Birds Pt. 1," the Drake feature on "The Zone"); *Echoes of Silence* extends into Michael Jackson territory with the "Dirty Diana" cover that closes the run. The Beach House sample on "Loft Music" was eventually cleared and the band reportedly negotiated terms for the *Trilogy* commercial release — one of the documented sample-clearance stories from the period.

We treat the three as one work because that is how the canon now treats them, and how *Trilogy* itself was packaged. The post-Trilogy Weeknd discography — *Beauty Behind the Madness*, *Starboy*, *After Hours*, *Dawn FM* — is more commercially significant and arguably more sonically polished, but the originating gesture is here. Scaruffi position extrapolated — has written about The Weeknd generally but specific Trilogy review not directly located in our sources.

##### 4. SZA — *Ctrl* (2017)

Fantano scored *Ctrl* 7/10 — solid for a TDE-affiliated R&B debut and a score he has subsequently revisited in retrospective coverage of SZA's career arc. The album reframed what PBR&B's second decade could sound like: less productionist-male-melancholy, more conversational-female-specific, drawing on early-2000s R&B and indie-pop production without sounding like either. SZA's voice — pitched between Lauryn Hill conversational and Jhené Aiko airy — carries songs that are essentially long-form text messages.

"Drew Barrymore," "The Weekend," "Love Galore" (with Travis Scott), "Broken Clocks," "Doves in the Wind" (with Kendrick Lamar), "Garden (Say It Like Dat)," and "Prom" — the singles cycle was unusually deep, and the album sustained chart presence for years. Lyrically *Ctrl* is interested in the specific texture of being in your mid-twenties and uncertain about everything: the title is ironic. The recorded voicemail interludes from SZA's mother and grandmother — telling her about discipline, self-respect, and the concept of control — are the structural device, framing the song-cycle as a generational dialogue rather than a personal monologue.

The production is more conventional than the Trilogy-era PBR&B template — drums are present, hooks resolve, songs are roughly four minutes long — but the songwriting smuggles in the genre's signature ambiguity. "The Weekend" is sung from the perspective of the side-piece in a polyamorous arrangement, with no judgment register. "Supermodel" opens the album with SZA singing about a partner who left her on Valentine's Day for a "lil-bitty white girl" — direct in a way the genre's male auteurs almost never were.

The follow-up *SOS* (2022) is the larger commercial moment — a fifteen-month chart presence and Grammy sweep — but the consensus critical position, Fantano included, treats *Ctrl* as the more focused statement. Its placement here reflects that *Ctrl* defined a second generation of the scene that the Trilogy-era male artists could not have written.

##### 5. FKA twigs — *MAGDALENE* (2019)

Fantano scored *MAGDALENE* 7/10 — qualified praise in which he credited the vocal performances and Kate Bush / Björk-form updates while noting the album was not quite greater than the sum of its parts. The album is twigs and producers (Nicolas Jaar, Skrillex, Koreless, Cashmere Cat, Benny Blanco, twigs herself) building a sound that incorporates classical, dembow, industrial, contemporary classical, and PBR&B production into something that no other artist has successfully matched.

Scaruffi has written approvingly about FKA twigs as one of the more interesting figures in 2010s art-pop; his engagement predates *MAGDALENE* but extends to it. *LP1* (2014) was the debut that put twigs in this conversation, and a real argument exists for *LP1* over *MAGDALENE* as the more genre-defining moment. We have placed *MAGDALENE* higher because it represents the artist at full formal command — the songs are stronger, the production more confident, the conceptual frame (post-Pattinson breakup, Mary Magdalene as figure) more cohesive.

"Cellophane," the closer, is the single most-discussed PBR&B song of the late decade — a piano ballad that twigs sings into near-silence, with production that arrives only in the bridge and then disappears. It is the closest the genre has come to art song. The music video, in which twigs performs an extended pole-dance choreography, was a major art-pop moment in its own right.

The album was made during and after a difficult period — uterine fibroid surgery, the end of her relationship with Robert Pattinson — and the lyric is more autobiographical than *LP1*'s persona-work. "Sad Day" (co-produced by twigs with Koreless, Benny Blanco, Cashmere Cat, Nicolas Jaar, Skrillex, and Noah Goldstein), "Holy Terrain" with Future as the album's sole featured guest, "Home With You," and the Skrillex co-production "Daybed" extend the texture. Twigs's vocal range — a fragile breathy upper register sliding into more conventional lower-mid R&B placement — is the through-line that holds the productionist diversity together.

##### 6. Solange — *A Seat at the Table* (2016)

Fantano scored *A Seat at the Table* 8/10. The genre tag is contested — Solange herself has resisted being filed as PBR&B, and the album draws more directly on neo-soul and art-pop than on the Trilogy lineage — but the brief explicitly includes it here, and we accept that placement.

The album is Solange's third LP and the first to find her in full artistic possession of her own project. Sonically it is closer to Joni Mitchell's *Court and Spark* than to any of her sister's records: arrangements built around organ, woodwinds, and unhurried vocal layering rather than drum programming. Sampha appears across multiple tracks. Q-Tip, Lil Wayne, and Master P contribute. The Master P interludes — spoken-word monologues about No Limit Records and Black autonomy — are the album's load-bearing structural device.

"Cranes in the Sky" won the Grammy for Best R&B Performance and is probably the most-played track, but the album is built to be heard whole. "Don't Touch My Hair" is the political center. "F.U.B.U." is the thesis statement.

Scaruffi position extrapolated — no Solange review specifically located in our sources, but his general framework (preference for art-pop and conceptual cohesion over commercial R&B) would predict approval. The album debuted at number one on the Billboard 200 — Solange's first — and was paired with a self-directed visual album for "Cranes in the Sky" and "Don't Touch My Hair" that doubled as a thesis on Black aesthetic autonomy.

Sonically the closest comparisons are Erykah Badu's *New Amerykah* records and Joni Mitchell's mid-1970s work; the rhythm section (Daniel Aged, Saul Williams, and others) is restrained almost to the point of disappearance, with the harmonic information carried by Rhodes piano, layered backing vocals, and woodwinds. Raphael Saadiq co-produced multiple tracks. The decision to include the Master P interludes — six of them, totaling over five minutes of spoken word — was the album's most-discussed structural choice and the one that pushes it furthest from PBR&B convention.

##### 7. James Blake — *James Blake* (2011)

The self-titled debut, released the same year as The Weeknd's first mixtape, is one of the genre's twin originating points — proof that the PBR&B sensibility was happening simultaneously in dubstep-adjacent London as in Toronto. Scaruffi has written about James Blake as one of the more serious electronic-music figures of the early 2010s; his engagement is documented even if specific per-album rankings are less so.

The album emerged from Blake's post-dubstep run (his earlier *CMYK* and *Klavierwerke* EPs are essential context) and reframed what a "singer-songwriter" record could be. Blake plays piano and sings in a multi-tracked falsetto over production built from sub-bass, micro-house glitches, and silence as a structural element. The Feist cover "Limit to Your Love" was the calling card; "I Never Learnt to Share" and "The Wilhelm Scream" extended the method.

The follow-up *Overgrown* (2013) is also widely admired — it won the Mercury Prize — and could plausibly take this slot; we have selected the debut because it is the genre-defining gesture. Blake's later collaborations with Beyoncé (*Lemonade*'s "Forward"), Frank Ocean (multiple *Blonde* credits), Travis Scott, Kendrick Lamar (*DAMN.* and *Mr. Morale*), and Jay-Z make him the most-credited single producer in this scene. The post-2018 Blake catalogue (*Assume Form*, *Friends That Break Your Heart*, the 2023 collaborations) has been more mixed critically, but the auteur status established by the debut and *Overgrown* is secure.

The technical innovation worth naming: Blake's vocal-processing approach, where his own voice is pitched, time-stretched, and re-arranged into chord-shaped fragments that function as both lead and harmony, became a standard production move across the scene by mid-decade. Producers like Mura Masa, Sevdaliza, and even mainstream-pop figures inherited the technique. The 2011 self-titled debut is where it first appears at album scale.

##### 8. Sampha — *Process* (2017)

Sampha was the in-demand session vocalist of the mid-2010s — Drake's "Too Much," Kanye's *Life of Pablo*, SBTRKT's debut, Solange's *A Seat at the Table* — before *Process* arrived in 2017 as his proper debut at age twenty-eight. The album won the Mercury Prize, which in this scene is a meaningful credential.

*Process* is constructed around grief: Sampha's mother died of cancer in 2015, and the album is essentially an extended response. "(No One Knows Me) Like the Piano" is the centerpiece — a piano ballad addressed to the family piano that became the household's stable point during his mother's illness. The production across the album is restrained, often sparse, with electronic textures that recede whenever the lyric requires foreground.

Fantano position extrapolated — *Process* is widely regarded as one of the strongest debuts in the scene's second wave, but a specific Needle Drop score is not in our verified list. Scaruffi position likewise not directly located. We include *Process* on the strength of broader critical consensus and its genre-defining lyrical-emotional register.

Sonically the album operates in the same productionist tradition as James Blake — Sampha was a Young Turks labelmate, and the two share both engineers and an approach to vocal layering — but the lyric is more directly emotional than Blake typically allows. "Blood On Me," the most rhythm-forward track, uses an industrial-tinged beat to dramatize an anxiety dream. "Plastic 100°C" begins with a sample of David Attenborough narrating a sunrise. "Incomplete Kisses" is the closest the album comes to a conventional R&B song. The whole record is forty-one minutes and ten tracks; the formal compression is part of the achievement.

##### 9. Daniel Caesar — *Freudian* (2017)

Fantano scored *Freudian* somewhere in the 7-8 range — the brief acknowledges this as approximate. *Freudian* is the album where Toronto's R&B scene proved it could produce someone other than The Weeknd or Drake: Caesar, working with producers Matthew Burnett and Jordan Evans, made a gospel-inflected, guitar-heavy debut that pulls more from Stevie Wonder and Maxwell than from the Trilogy lineage.

"Get You" (with Kali Uchis) and "Best Part" (with H.E.R., which won the 2019 Grammy for Best R&B Performance) are the breakout singles. The album sustained chart presence for two years and turned Caesar into one of the few traditional-songwriter figures in a scene dominated by productionists. The vocal arrangements — multi-tracked harmonies pulling from Black church traditions — are the load-bearing element.

The placement is the lowest top-ten slot, reflecting that *Freudian* is closer to the genre's neo-soul edge than its productionist center — a real argument could be made for placing it in our Volume 3 neo-soul file instead. Caesar's subsequent records (*Case Study 01* in 2019, *Never Enough* in 2023) have been more mixed critically; *Freudian* remains the high point and the album that established the post-Frank Ocean Black-male-songwriter template that artists from Brent Faiyaz to Giveon would inherit.

Worth noting: Caesar grew up in Oshawa, Ontario in a Seventh-day Adventist household and reportedly left home at seventeen after a falling-out with his father over secular music. The gospel inflection across *Freudian* — particularly on "Hold Me Down" and the closing title track — is autobiographical rather than affectation.

##### 10. The Weeknd — *After Hours* (2020)

The coin-flip survivor: *After Hours* (2020) versus *Beauty Behind the Madness* (2015) versus *Dawn FM* (2022) as the Weeknd's best post-Trilogy work. Coin flip resolved in favor of *After Hours*, partly on the strength of "Blinding Lights" (the longest-charting Hot 100 single in history) and partly because the album represents the cleanest synthesis of Trilogy-era atmosphere with the 1980s synth-pop register that the Weeknd would fully inhabit on *Dawn FM*.

Fantano position on *After Hours* is mixed-positive, leaning toward documented appreciation of the production work; specific score not in our verified list. Scaruffi position not directly located. The placement reflects that the Weeknd's post-Trilogy mainstream peak is significant enough to warrant double-canonization, with Trilogy as the originating gesture and *After Hours* as the mature commercial apex.

The album is also the genre's most-streamed work and its most successful crossover into pop-rock listenership; "Save Your Tears," "In Your Eyes," and "Heartless" extend the single run. The Max Martin co-productions are unusually integrated for someone of Martin's profile — Tesfaye remains the auteur. The visual concept (bloodied face, red suit, the recurring "After Hours" character introduced via the SNL "Blinding Lights" performance and the *Trois Fois Par Jour* mini-films) is the most sustained transmedia run of the Weeknd's career.

The cinematic Daft Punk influence on the album's synth palette is explicit — Daft Punk's *Random Access Memories* had been a 2013 reference for *Starboy* (where Daft Punk themselves co-wrote and produced the title track), and *After Hours* extends that lineage into the Italo-disco and early-Madonna register that *Dawn FM* would fully commit to. "Hardest to Love" and "Faith" sit at the album's emotional center; the Tarkovsky-via-Lynch closing track "Until I Bleed Out" is the album's most-overlooked moment.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

Two irreconcilable disputes survived the debate and were resolved by coin flip rather than further argument:

**FKA twigs *LP1* (2014) vs. *MAGDALENE* (2019).** Scaruffi's framework slightly favors *LP1* as the more genre-defining gesture; Fantano's documented 7/10 attached to *MAGDALENE* and his prior coverage of *LP1* sit in roughly the same territory, so this dispute did not resolve on score-deltas. The coin came up *MAGDALENE*. *LP1* moves to honorable mentions; this is the closest call on the list and reasonable canons could place *LP1* in the top ten and bump our number-ten Weeknd slot.

**The Weeknd *After Hours* vs. *Beauty Behind the Madness* vs. *Dawn FM*.** Three-way coin flip required (we used a three-sided die metaphorically — die was three sequential flips, *After Hours* won). *Beauty Behind the Madness* has the larger pop moment ("Can't Feel My Face," "The Hills") but is less cohesive; *Dawn FM* is the more conceptually unified late-period work but commercially smaller. *After Hours* sits between them and won on that synthesis logic. Both losing albums move to honorable mentions.

#### Honorable Mentions

- **FKA twigs — *LP1* (2014).** Lost the *MAGDALENE* coin flip; the debut that put twigs in the conversation. "Two Weeks" remains the single.
- **The Weeknd — *Beauty Behind the Madness* (2015).** Lost the post-Trilogy coin flip. The commercial breakthrough; "Can't Feel My Face" and "The Hills" defined a year of pop radio.
- **The Weeknd — *Dawn FM* (2022).** Lost the post-Trilogy coin flip. The more conceptually unified late work, with Jim Carrey as DJ-narrator. A real argument for top ten.
- **James Blake — *Overgrown* (2013).** The follow-up that won the Mercury Prize. "Retrograde" is the single. Could replace the debut on certain canon constructions.
- **Solange — *When I Get Home* (2019).** The Houston-themed follow-up to *A Seat at the Table*; less immediately legible but more formally adventurous.
- **Kelela — *Take Me Apart* (2017).** Arca, Kingdom, and Jam City production; one of the scene's purest fusions with electronic music.
- **Miguel — *Kaleidoscope Dream* (2012) / *Wildheart* (2015).** The genre's most underrated guitar player and arguably its most genuine Prince inheritor.
- **Blood Orange — *Cupid Deluxe* (2013) / *Freetown Sound* (2016).** Dev Hynes's records sit at the indie-rock/PBR&B border and have a real claim to canonization.
- **Jhené Aiko — *Souled Out* (2014) / *Trip* (2017).** The genre's most consistent female vocalist of the early-mid 2010s.
- **Bryson Tiller — *T R A P S O U L* (2015).** The mixtape-aesthetic Atlanta-meets-Toronto crossover.
- **Jeremih — *Late Nights: The Album* (2015).** Chicago R&B and Mick Schultz production; underrated as a song-cycle.
- **How to Dress Well — *Love Remains* (2010).** Tom Krell's debut, one of the genre's founding lo-fi gestures; predates Trilogy by a year.
- **Snoh Aalegra — *Ugh, those feels again* (2019).** The Swedish-Iranian late-decade entry; the Sade comparison is earned.
- **NAO — *For All We Know* (2016).** UK alt-R&B; "funky-wonky" as she has called it.
- **Kehlani — *SweetSexySavage* (2017).** The mixtape-to-album transition done well.
- **dvsn — *Sept. 5th* (2016).** OVO Sound's Trilogy-aesthetic late entry.

The genre as constructed here covers roughly 2010 to 2022 — from How to Dress Well's *Love Remains* and the Weeknd Trilogy through *Dawn FM* and *SOS*. Whether anything released after closes this canon meaningfully remains an open question; for now, the ten above are the load-bearing entries.

One final caveat on critical attribution: PBR&B is the file in this volume where Fantano's documented coverage is densest and Scaruffi's is thinnest. Where we have cited Fantano scores, those are the numbers from his published Needle Drop reviews as best as we have been able to verify them (Blonde 8/10, Channel Orange "Strong 7," MAGDALENE 7/10, Ctrl 7/10, A Seat at the Table 8/10, Freudian 7-8 territory). Worth flagging explicitly: Fantano's PBR&B scores tend to sit a notch lower than the volume's canonical placements would suggest — a useful reminder that critical canonization and Needle Drop scores are not the same exercise. Where we have invoked Scaruffi without direct citation, we have flagged the extrapolation. Readers should treat the Fantano-positions as documented and the Scaruffi-positions on artists beyond Frank Ocean, FKA twigs, James Blake, and the Weeknd as our inference from his general framework rather than direct sourcing.

---

## Part Two — Funk

Seven sub-genres, roughly thirty years of generative work and a continuing tail into the present. The arc this part covers runs from James Brown's mid-1960s reorganization of his band around the rhythm section — the moment that conventionally dates funk as a distinct form, with "Out of Sight" (1964), "Papa's Got a Brand New Bag" (1965), and "Cold Sweat" (1967) as the foundational singles — through the Prince-led 1980s Minneapolis Sound synthesis that recombines funk with new-wave pop and proto-electro and produces the form's most commercially successful late-period iteration. Inside that arc the form does almost everything Black American popular music can do at the level of rhythm-first composition: it builds the JB-tradition classic funk vocabulary in the late 1960s and early 1970s; it generates Parliament-Funkadelic's cosmic theatre as funk-as-total-artwork; it consolidates the Washington-DC go-go tradition as a regional rhythm continuum that resists commercial portability; it builds the boogie / electro-funk wing of the early 1980s around synthesizers and drum machines; it generates the funk-rock collision with electric guitar; it produces the jazz-funk crossover that cross-references heavily with Volume 2's soul-jazz and fusion files; and it ends in the Minneapolis Sound's pop-funk synthesis that fuses funk's rhythmic architecture with new-wave production and broad chart presence.

The structural argument that organizes this part is that funk is the moment Black American popular music decides the band is the composer. The soul tradition that fed it kept the singer at the center; the funk tradition that emerges in the late 1960s treats the singer as one element of a larger groove architecture in which the rhythm section, the horn section, and (eventually) the synthesizer-and-drum-machine apparatus are the primary artistic agents. James Brown's own description of the shift is famously simple — he started "putting it all on the one," meaning the downbeat became the consistent rhythmic anchor and everything else was free to syncopate against it — and the description fits the music more cleanly than most musician-self-descriptions do. The seven sub-genres in this part are seven distinct answers to the question of what kind of rhythm-section architecture produces what kind of music. The answers do not collapse into each other.

**Classic Funk** is the JB tradition and its immediate descendants. James Brown's late-1960s and early-1970s catalog with the JBs (Bootsy Collins and his brother Catfish on bass and guitar before they joined Parliament-Funkadelic, Fred Wesley on trombone, Maceo Parker on alto saxophone, the long sequence of drummers including Clyde Stubblefield and John "Jabo" Starks), the Sly and the Family Stone catalog from *Stand!* (1969) through *There's a Riot Goin' On* (1971) and *Fresh* (1973), the broader 1970s funk catalog — the Meters in New Orleans, Kool and the Gang's pre-disco early-1970s records, the Ohio Players, Mandrill, the Bar-Kays after the 1967 Otis Redding plane crash, the Average White Band's Scottish-via-Atlantic funk, the Brothers Johnson, Cameo's early records, Slave, Lakeside, Con Funk Shun, Brick, Fatback — constitutes the file's body. The form's centers of gravity are the Brown catalog and the Sly Stone catalog, with the secondary acts working various points along the spectrum between Brown's horn-driven minimalism and Sly's denser psychedelic-influenced arrangements. The cross-listings into psychedelic soul (where Sly Stone primarily anchors) and into P-Funk (where Bootsy Collins crossed over) are flagged.

**P-Funk** is George Clinton's cosmic theatre. Parliament and Funkadelic — two bands sharing a personnel base around Clinton, Bernie Worrell on keyboards, Bootsy Collins on bass, Eddie Hazel on lead guitar, and a rotating roster of vocalists including Glenn Goins, Garry Shider, Ray Davis, Calvin Simon, Grady Thomas, Fuzzy Haskins, and the Brides of Funkenstein and Parlet vocal groups — produced through the 1970s and into the early 1980s the most ambitious body of work in the volume. Parliament as the horn-driven funk-as-spectacle band (the *Mothership Connection* mythology, *The Clones of Dr. Funkenstein*, *Funkentelechy vs. the Placebo Syndrome*, *Motor Booty Affair*), Funkadelic as the guitar-driven psychedelic-rock-meets-funk band (*Maggot Brain*, *America Eats Its Young*, *Cosmic Slop*, *Standing on the Verge of Getting It On*, *Let's Take It to the Stage*, *One Nation Under a Groove*), the Bootsy's Rubber Band offshoot, the Brides of Funkenstein records, the Parlet records, the late catalog through *The Electric Spanking of War Babies* (1981) — the file works through what is functionally a single artistic project under two band names with multiple satellite identities. Both critics rate P-Funk extremely high; the disputes are about which records inside the catalog to canonize, not about the catalog's importance.

**Go-Go** is the Washington-DC rhythm continuum. From roughly 1976 (Chuck Brown and the Soul Searchers' *Bustin' Loose*, the form's foundational record) through the present, the go-go tradition has functioned as a regional rhythm-and-percussion-driven funk variant that does not work outside the live-performance context the way the broader funk catalog does. Trouble Funk, Rare Essence, Experience Unlimited (E.U.), the Junkyard Band, Backyard Band — the form is built around extended grooves, call-and-response interactions between band and audience, and a percussion-heavy rhythm-section vocabulary (timbales, cowbell, multiple congas) that connects it as much to Latin music as to the broader funk tradition. The file is the most regionally-specific in the volume and the one where the album-as-unit-of-analysis fits worst — the canonical go-go documents are live records and the studio catalog is thinner. Both critics' coverage is sparse and the file is the most editor-driven in the funk section.

**Boogie / Electro-Funk** is the synth-and-machine 1980s. From roughly 1979 (Vaughan Mason and Crew's "Bounce, Rock, Skate, Roll," Sister Sledge's *We Are Family*, the early Patrick Adams productions for Bumblebee Unlimited and Inner Life) through the mid-1980s, a wing of the funk tradition reorganized around the Roland TR-808 drum machine, the synthesizer bass (Moog, Prophet-5, Oberheim), and a brighter, more streamlined production aesthetic. The file works through the boogie catalog (Kashif, the early Whitney Houston productions before she pivoted to pop balladry, the late Kool and the Gang records, the SOS Band, the Mary Jane Girls, Cherrelle, the post-disco crossover material) and the electro-funk wing (Afrika Bambaataa and Soulsonic Force's *Planet Rock* sessions, the early Hashim, Newcleus, Egyptian Lover, Jonzun Crew material, Zapp and Roger Troutman's vocoder-and-talkbox funk). The form bleeds into early hip-hop and into the disco-and-dance file in Part Three, and the cross-listings are dense.

**Funk-Rock** is the guitar collision. From roughly 1969 (the early Funkadelic catalog, the post-Hendrix Band of Gypsys material, the Sly Stone *Stand!* sessions) forward, a wing of the funk tradition fused with electric-rock guitar and rock-era production to produce a hybrid form. Funkadelic when it anchors more in the rock-funk pole than in the P-Funk-cosmic pole, the Isley Brothers' T-Neck records from the late-1960s Brothers Isley sessions through *3 + 3* (1973) and *The Heat Is On* (1975), the Bar-Kays in their funk-rock mode, the broader 1970s funk-rock catalog (War, Mandrill, Mother's Finest, Graham Central Station, Slave, Cameo's guitar-funk material), the Fishbone and Living Colour and Red Hot Chili Peppers continuation into the 1980s, and Lenny Kravitz and D'Angelo's *Voodoo* on the contemporary tail. The cross-listings into Volume 4's rock files are dense; the boundary call (does the record anchor here or in rock?) is made on the basis of where the rhythmic vocabulary sits, with funk-grounded records anchoring in this file and rock-grounded records anchoring in Volume 4.

**Jazz-Funk** is the instrumental crossover. From roughly 1972 (Donald Byrd's *Black Byrd*, Herbie Hancock's *Headhunters* the following year) through the late 1970s, a wing of the post-bop jazz generation moved into funk vocabulary and produced records that anchor for some listeners as jazz and for others as funk. Herbie Hancock's electric-period catalog from *Headhunters* (1973) through *Man-Child* (1975) and *Secrets* (1976), Donald Byrd's Mizell-brothers-produced run on Blue Note (*Black Byrd*, *Street Lady*, *Stepping into Tomorrow*, *Places and Spaces*), Roy Ayers Ubiquity's catalog (*Everybody Loves the Sunshine*, *Mystic Voyage*, *Vibrations*), the CTI label's late catalog under Creed Taylor, Grover Washington Jr.'s *Mister Magic* (1975) and *Winelight* (1980), George Duke's electric-period work, the Brothers Johnson, the Lonnie Liston Smith catalog — the file works through the body of records that anchor in this volume primarily because they were marketed and rated as funk records even though the musicians came out of the jazz infrastructure. The cross-listings into Volume 2's soul-jazz and fusion files are the densest in the entire project; the working rule is that records made primarily for jazz-club audiences anchor in Volume 2, records made primarily for funk-radio audiences anchor here.

**Minneapolis Sound** is the Prince-led synthesis. From roughly 1980 (Prince's *Dirty Mind*) through the late 1980s, Prince Rogers Nelson and his Minneapolis-based satellite catalog (the Time, Vanity 6, Apollonia 6, Sheila E. as a related but California-based figure, the post-Prince Jam and Lewis productions for the SOS Band and Janet Jackson and Cherrelle, Jesse Johnson's solo records, Alexander O'Neal) produced a body of work that fused funk's rhythmic architecture with new-wave synthesizer production, glam-pop stage presentation, and broad chart presence. Prince's run from *Dirty Mind* (1980) through *Controversy* (1981), *1999* (1982), *Purple Rain* (1984), *Around the World in a Day* (1985), *Parade* (1986), *Sign o' the Times* (1987), and the *Black Album* / *Lovesexy* (1987-1988) is the file's central artifact-cluster, and the deepest single-artist run in the volume — Stevie Wonder's classic period in Part One is the only comparable run. The Time's first three records (*The Time*, *What Time Is It?*, *Ice Cream Castle*) function as Prince's covert second band. The Jam and Lewis productions for Janet Jackson (*Control* 1986, *Rhythm Nation 1814* 1989) extend the Minneapolis Sound into the pop-R&B mainstream. The file is heavily Prince-weighted in a way that is unavoidable — the form does not exist without him.

Seven sub-genres. The arc they describe is the rhythm-first phase of the volume's three-phase argument. Reading them in order is the right way through. The dependencies run forward — P-Funk reads against classic funk, boogie reads against the funk tradition that preceded it, the Minneapolis Sound reads against everything before it — and the cross-references into Part One (where soul ends and funk begins) and Part Three (where funk feeds into disco and post-disco) are dense and flagged.

---

### Classic / Early Funk
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

The argument over Classic Funk — the lineage that begins with James Brown's Famous Flames-era band tightening into a rhythm machine in 1965, runs through New Orleans with the Meters, gets stretched into jazz-fusion shapes by Kool & the Gang and War, and crests with Earth, Wind & Fire's universalist 1975-77 run — is, between these two critics, a debate about whether funk's center is rhythmic or compositional. Scaruffi is unambiguous: funk is a rhythmic revolution, and James Brown is the revolutionary. He treats *Live at the Apollo* as a documentary of pre-funk soul tipping over the edge, but reserves his highest praise for the JB band's instrumental sides — *Sex Machine*, the late-60s singles compiled on *In the Jungle Groove*, *The Payback* — where the groove itself becomes the composition. He has long admired The Meters' first three Josie LPs as the second great funk lineage: shorter, drier, more Caribbean than the JB band, but with the same idea that the rhythm section IS the song. EWF's *That's the Way of the World* he respects; everything more grandiose he tolerates less.

Fantano, born 1985, came to all of this in retrospect, and his sensibility leans toward the maximal, melodically rich, polished end of the canon. We can credibly extrapolate that he leans hard on EWF's mid-70s peak (*That's the Way of the World*, *All 'n All*, *Gratitude*), the Isley Brothers' 3+3 era as a guitar-rock-and-soul hybrid, and the Meters' *Rejuvenation*. He has publicly shown enthusiasm for the JB canon but treats the compilations (*Star Time*, *In the Jungle Groove*) as the access point rather than any single LP. **All Fantano-specific picks below are extrapolated from his stated tastes and FLAGGED.**

The arguments that actually moved the list: (1) how many JB titles get in — Scaruffi wanted four, Fantano two; (2) whether Tower of Power's white-funk professionalism belongs in a top 10 at all — Fantano yes, Scaruffi no; (3) whether *Wild and Peaceful* or *Light of Worlds* represents Kool & the Gang's peak — split decision; (4) The Isleys' *3+3* vs *The Heat Is On* — Fantano's call broke it; (5) War's *The World Is a Ghetto* vs *Why Can't We Be Friends?* — settled by coin flip after four rounds of stalemate.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. James Brown — *Live at the Apollo* (King, 1963)

Recorded October 24, 1962 at the Apollo Theater in Harlem and released the following spring on King Records, this is the document on which both critics start, even if neither calls it the most "funk" record in the JB catalog. Scaruffi's framing is correct: in 1962 Brown was still primarily a soul-revue bandleader, and *Live at the Apollo* captures the moment before the rhythmic revolution — the breath before "Out of Sight" and "Papa's Got a Brand New Bag" — when the gospel-derived showmanship had been honed to a knife's edge but the band hadn't yet been redirected from chord changes to the One. Yet the record belongs at the top of any Classic Funk list because every later JB innovation is latent in it: the bandleader-as-conductor barking cues, the horn section operating as a single percussive voice, the medley structure that treats songs as zones to enter and exit rather than discrete units, and most of all the absolute primacy of the downbeat.

The performance is short — barely over half an hour — and built around the ballad complex "Lost Someone" and the closing medley, which gives a generation of subsequent live albums their template. Famous Flames vocal interplay is at its peak; Brown's exhortation work ("Are you with me? Are you with me?") establishes the rhetorical mode he would use for the next twenty-five years. Both critics treat *Live at the Apollo* as canonical and uncontroversial, and on this they agree before they agree on almost anything else. Scaruffi places it as the launching point of modern Black music in the post-Cooke era; Fantano (extrapolated) treats it as a top-tier live document in any genre. Position one is non-negotiable.

##### 2. James Brown — *In the Jungle Groove* (Polydor, 1986)

Strictly speaking a compilation, assembled by Cliff White and released on Polydor in 1986, *In the Jungle Groove* is treated by Scaruffi as effectively the canonical JB instrumental funk album because it gathers the 1969-1970 sessions — including extended and previously unedited versions of "Funky Drummer," "Give It Up or Turnit a Loose," "Talkin' Loud and Sayin' Nothing," and "Get Up, Get Into It, Get Involved" — that constitute the most-sampled, most-influential rhythm tracks in recorded music. The personnel are the documented JB band of that period: Clyde Stubblefield on drums for "Funky Drummer," Jabo Starks on others, Bobby Byrd's backing vocals, Maceo Parker and Fred Wesley anchoring the horns, with Bootsy and Catfish Collins arriving in the 1970 lineup that produced "Sex Machine."

What makes this compilation rather than any single contemporary studio LP the right pick is editorial: White restored the unedited eight-minute "Funky Drummer" and put the related material in conversation with itself, which is the only way the JB band's modular, take-after-take working method makes sense as an "album." Scaruffi treats the rhythm tracks here as the foundational text of funk. Fantano (extrapolated) almost certainly prefers *Star Time* as the more complete career overview, but for a strict Classic Funk canon, *In the Jungle Groove* is the tighter argument. The record is also the cleanest answer to the question "what is funk, exactly?" — strip away the vocals and what remains is the answer, and what remains here is what hip-hop spent the next twenty years sampling.

##### 3. James Brown — *Sex Machine* (King, 1970)

Released in summer 1970 on King Records as a double LP, *Sex Machine* is the album-length JB statement that both critics can agree to put in a top three. The record is famously not entirely live — the first LP, recorded in studio with overdubbed crowd noise, presents the new Bootsy-and-Catfish Collins lineup that had been with Brown for only a few months, debuting the title track and stretching "Brother Rapp" and "I Got the Feelin'" into vehicle-length workouts. The second LP is genuinely live, recorded at Bell Auditorium in Augusta, Georgia, with the prior band. The cognitive dissonance between the two halves is itself instructive: you hear the rhythm-section pivot in real time.

Scaruffi treats *Sex Machine* as the definitive transitional document — the moment funk's signature One-and-three accent pattern, articulated by Bootsy's bass and the Collins brothers' interlock, becomes fully audible as a new musical language rather than a stylistic tendency. The title track itself, with its call-and-response between Brown and Bobby Byrd ("Get on up — get on up"), is the most-quoted JB cue after "Hit me!" and the structure of the recording — long, modal, vamp-based, with the bandleader directing changes in real time — would be exported wholesale into the dance music of the next half-century. Fantano (extrapolated) ranks this slightly lower than *In the Jungle Groove* on overall cohesion but acknowledges the documentary value. Position three reflects compromise.

##### 4. The Meters — *Look-Ka Py Py* (Josie, 1969)

The Meters' second LP, released on Josie Records in early 1970 and recorded in 1969 in New Orleans, is the record both critics put forward as the alternative center of gravity to the JB lineage. The lineup — Art Neville on organ and electric piano, Leo Nocentelli on guitar, George Porter Jr. on bass, Joseph "Zigaboo" Modeliste on drums — is one of the great rhythm sections in recorded music, and *Look-Ka Py Py* is its driest, most instrumental statement. There are vocals, but they are chant-like, secondary to the interlocking pattern work; the band's signature is what Nocentelli has called the "second line" feel of New Orleans parade rhythm reorganized as small-combo funk.

Scaruffi has been writing about *Look-Ka Py Py* approvingly for decades, treating it as evidence that funk was not solely a JB-band invention but had a parallel Caribbean-inflected origin in New Orleans. The record is short — most tracks barely over two minutes — and the brevity itself is a thesis: where the JB band stretched grooves into modal expanses, the Meters compressed them into riff-objects, each track a self-contained machine. Modeliste's drumming, with its displaced backbeat and ghost-note vocabulary, has been studied as carefully as Clyde Stubblefield's. Fantano (extrapolated) probably places *Rejuvenation* slightly higher in his personal ranking, but *Look-Ka Py Py* is the consensus selection for the band's archetypal early statement, and position four reflects both critics' agreement.

##### 5. Earth, Wind & Fire — *That's the Way of the World* (Columbia, 1975)

Released March 1975 on Columbia, *That's the Way of the World* is the EWF album that both critics, working from very different starting positions, place in the top five. Scaruffi's respect is real if measured: he treats this record as the most successful integration of African polyrhythm, jazz harmony, soul vocal tradition, and pop song form in the 1970s, and credits Maurice White's compositional ambition without endorsing the more grandiose statements that came after.
