Compass Point gets two slots. Mainstream pop gets three. Freestyle gets one. The big absences — Tom Tom Club, Mtume, Yarbrough & Peoples, Taana Gardner — sit in honorable mentions, all of them genuinely defensible on a different day.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Arthur Russell — *World of Echo* (1986)

Both critics' number one, and the only album on this list that neither of them argued about. Released in 1986 on Upside/Rough Trade, *World of Echo* is Arthur Russell alone with a cello, his voice, and a tape delay — the dance producer who made *24->24 Music* and "Is It All Over My Face?" turning inward and producing what is effectively a solo voice-and-cello song cycle dissolved in reverb. It does not sound like post-disco in any superficial sense. There are no four-on-the-floor kicks, no horn stabs, no diva vocals. But it is the secret center of the entire post-disco project, because it makes audible the thing that Russell was always doing under the dance tracks: an obsession with overtones, with the way a single sustained note can become a chord through reflection, with the human voice as just another resonant object.

Scaruffi places Russell in the lineage of his favorite minimalists — Riley, Young, the early downtown drone composers — and treats *World of Echo* as the album where that lineage finally produced a pop voice. Fantano's engagement is different; his appreciation came through the 2000s reissue cycle, and his framing is closer to ambient/folk than to minimalism. Both are right. The record contains drumless versions of "Let's Go Swimming," "Treehouse," and "Wax the Van" that strip those dance tracks down to skeleton and shadow — proof that the songs were always songs, not just grooves with vocals attached. It is one of the few records on this list that rewards headphone listening at low volume more than any club system. **A cellist whispering into a tape delay accidentally invented bedroom pop a decade before bedroom pop.**

##### 2. Grace Jones — *Nightclubbing* (1981)

Compass Point Studios, Nassau, Bahamas. Chris Blackwell producing. Sly Dunbar and Robbie Shakespeare on drums and bass. Wally Badarou on keyboards. Barry Reynolds and Mikey Chung on guitars. Uziah "Sticky" Thompson on percussion. This is the verifiable lineup, and it is the most important house band of the post-disco era — a multinational rhythm section that fused dub reggae, new wave, funk, and the residual pulse of disco into something that did not previously exist. *Nightclubbing* is the middle entry of the Compass Point trilogy (*Warm Leatherette* in 1980, *Nightclubbing* in 1981, *Living My Life* in 1982) and the one where the formula reaches full power.

The covers tell the story: Iggy Pop's "Nightclubbing," Sting's "Demolition Man," Bill Withers' "Use Me," Astor Piazzolla's "Libertango" reworked as "I've Seen That Face Before." Jones is not a virtuoso singer in the conventional sense, and the Compass Point band is what makes the record work — but her instinct for what to do with a song, how to flatten its melody into a recitation and let the rhythm carry the meaning, is the post-disco vocal stance in its purest form. "Pull Up to the Bumper" is the obvious single and one of the great dance tracks of the decade, but the album's center is "I've Seen That Face Before (Libertango)," which is essentially a tango played by a reggae rhythm section with a Jamaican-American-French chanteuse on top, and somehow it works. Scaruffi rates it among the best records of the 1980s. Fantano's appreciation is retrospective but real; he has talked about the Compass Point sound as one of the most undervalued production schools of the era. Agreement was immediate.

##### 3. Dinosaur L — *24->24 Music* (1981)

The other Arthur Russell entry, and the one that makes the dance-music case for him most directly. Released on Sleeping Bag Records in 1981 under his Dinosaur L alias, *24->24 Music* is the album that contains "Go Bang! #5," the track that Walter Gibbons remixed into one of the foundational records of New York club music. The full album is stranger than the remix suggests — long instrumental passages, free-jazz horn interjections, vocal cut-ups, Russell's cello buried in the mix doing things that don't quite resolve harmonically. It is dance music made by someone who didn't entirely believe in dance music, and that ambivalence is what makes it permanent.

Scaruffi treats this as one of the canonical New York avant-disco records, in the lineage that runs from the loft scene through the Paradise Garage and into early house. Fantano's relationship is more abstract — he came to Russell through *World of Echo* and *Calling Out of Context*, and *24->24 Music* is a slightly harder sell because it requires you to care about the original tracks rather than the curated comps. Both agreed it belongs in the top three. The reason is structural: this is the album where you can hear the bridge from disco to house being built in real time, by someone who didn't know he was building it. Larry Levan played "Go Bang!" at the Garage. Frankie Knuckles took the same DNA to Chicago. The line from this record to Larry Heard is shorter than it looks.

##### 4. Michael Jackson — *Off the Wall* (1979)

The coin-flip slot, and the more defensible of the two Jackson options. *Off the Wall* is 1979 — technically the last year of disco rather than the first year of post-disco — but its release sits exactly on the genre's hinge point, and its sound (the Quincy Jones production, the Rod Temperton songs, the live rhythm section playing disco rhythms with rock-band precision) is what disco became after the backlash. "Don't Stop 'Til You Get Enough" is a disco track. "Rock with You" is post-disco — slower, more polished, more melodic, with a production aesthetic that points forward to the 1980s rather than backward to the 1970s.

Scaruffi is not a Jackson partisan and would have left both *Off the Wall* and *Thriller* off the list entirely if pressed. Fantano's pop-canonical instincts demanded a Jackson entry, and his case for *Off the Wall* over *Thriller* is the one Scaruffi accepted: *Off the Wall* is more of a piece, less compromised by the rock crossover impulse, and its rhythm section work (Louis Johnson on bass, John Robinson on drums, Greg Phillinganes on keys) is among the best of the era. **Fantano is extrapolated here — his actual coverage of pre-1985 pop is thin, and his pick of *Off the Wall* over *Thriller* is inferred from his general preference for the well-made record over the culturally-dominant one.** *Thriller* lost the coin flip because *Thriller* is a pop album that happens to have post-disco tracks on it, while *Off the Wall* is a post-disco album that happens to have crossed over.

##### 5. Liquid Liquid — *Liquid Liquid* (1981)

Technically an EP compilation, but treated as an album by both critics and by the genre's historiography. Liquid Liquid were a downtown New York four-piece who played a kind of percussion-and-bass dance music that had almost no antecedents — no guitar, just bass (Richard McGuire, doubling on keyboards), drums (Scott Hartley), marimba and keyboards (Dennis Young), and voice (Sal Principato), with all four members shading into percussion, playing repetitive funk-derived grooves with no-wave intensity. Released on 99 Records in 1981, the self-titled EP contains "Cavern," the track whose bassline was lifted (uncredited, then credited after a lawsuit) by Sugar Hill for "White Lines" by Grandmaster Melle Mel.

This was the coin-flip-winning slot, edging out Loose Joints' "Is It All Over My Face?" — which is the more important single but does not exist as an album. Scaruffi puts Liquid Liquid in the canonical no-wave/dance-punk lineage alongside ESG, with both bands representing the moment when downtown art-rock discovered that funk's rhythmic vocabulary was deeper than punk's. Fantano's coverage of Liquid Liquid is retrospective and brief, but his general orientation toward bass-and-rhythm minimalism (his appreciation of post-punk, his coverage of bands like ESG-adjacent revivalists) suggests he would land in the same place. The record is short, repetitive, and almost ascetic — three musicians and a singer playing two-bar loops for the entire side — and it is one of the foundational documents of what would eventually be called dance-punk.

##### 6. ESG — *Come Away with ESG* (1983)

The Bronx-via-99-Records counterpart to Liquid Liquid, and the more durable record of the two. ESG were four sisters (and a friend) — Renee, Marie, Valerie, and Deborah Scroggins, with Tito Libran on percussion — who played a kind of stripped-down funk that owed equally to James Brown, no wave, and proto-hip-hop. *Come Away with ESG* came out on 99 Records in 1983, after the band had already become a club fixture through their first EP and through "UFO," a track that has been sampled more times than almost any single in hip-hop history.

The album is short, rhythm-forward, and built almost entirely around bass and percussion, with Renee Scroggins' deadpan vocals on top. "Dance" and "Moody" are the highlights. The production is dry to the point of austerity — there are almost no overdubs, no studio effects, just the band playing live with very little ornamentation. Scaruffi treats ESG as foundational to the dance-punk lineage and to the post-disco continuum more broadly; he has written about them in the context of both no wave and early hip-hop. Fantano's coverage is limited but appreciative — ESG fits cleanly into his framework of bass-heavy, rhythm-first underground music with cross-genre influence. Both lists had them. The argument was about which ESG record — the first EP versus *Come Away with ESG* — and the album won on length and consistency.

##### 7. Madonna — *Like a Virgin* (1984)

The Madonna slot, and the one where Fantano's pop instincts overruled Scaruffi's preference. Scaruffi's pick was the 1983 debut *Madonna*, which is the more genre-canonical post-disco record — Reggie Lucas and Jellybean Benitez producing, tracks like "Holiday" and "Lucky Star" sitting right at the freestyle/post-disco border. *Like a Virgin* is Nile Rodgers producing, which gives it a different lineage (Chic, Diana Ross's *diana*, the post-disco that came out of the disco architects rather than the post-disco kids). The title track and "Material Girl" are pop singles first and dance records second.

Fantano's case was that *Like a Virgin* is the more important album culturally, that Rodgers' production gives it a sonic identity the debut lacks, and that "Into the Groove" — added to later pressings and to the *Desperately Seeking Susan* soundtrack — is one of the great post-disco singles of the decade. Scaruffi conceded reluctantly. **Fantano's pick is extrapolated here; his coverage of pre-2007 Madonna is thin, and the preference for *Like a Virgin* over *Madonna* is inferred from his pop-canonical instincts rather than from a documented review.** The flag is real. But the argument for the Rodgers record over the debut is the stronger argument, and the list reflects that.

##### 8. Janet Jackson — *Control* (1986)

The latest entry on the list, and the album that closes the post-disco era by inventing what comes next. *Control* is Jimmy Jam and Terry Lewis producing — both former members of the Time, both schooled in the Minneapolis funk-meets-new-wave aesthetic of early Prince — and the record's drum programming, synth bass lines, and vocal arrangements are the bridge from post-disco to new jack swing. "What Have You Done for Me Lately," "Nasty," "Control," "When I Think of You" — these are not disco tracks, but they descend directly from post-disco's rhythmic vocabulary, and the production aesthetic (LinnDrum, DX7, sequenced bass) is the post-disco aesthetic in its fully digital form.

Fantano's case for *Control* was straightforward — it is one of the most important pop records of the 1980s and the moment when R&B production caught up to and overtook pop production. Scaruffi was skeptical, preferring records earlier in the timeline, but conceded that *Control* belongs as the closing entry, the album that demonstrates what post-disco's rhythmic innovations sounded like when fully absorbed into mainstream pop. **Fantano's preference for *Control* over later Janet records (*Rhythm Nation* etc.) is reasonable but extrapolated.** The pick stands because the album is genuinely foundational and because no list of 1980s dance-pop records is complete without it.

##### 9. Was (Not Was) — *Born to Laugh at Tornadoes* (1983)

The Ze Records adjacent slot, representing the broader downtown New York / Detroit weird-funk axis that Ze Records (Cristina, Kid Creole, James Chance) defined. Was (Not Was) were the Detroit duo of Don and David Was, joined by vocalists Sweet Pea Atkinson and Sir Harry Bowens, making a kind of mutant funk that incorporated jazz horns, art-rock guests (Mel Tormé, Mitch Ryder, Ozzy Osbourne all appear on this album), and a slippery sense of irony that was part of the downtown sensibility. *Born to Laugh at Tornadoes* is their 1983 second album, on Ze/Geffen.

Scaruffi has consistently included Was (Not Was) in his canonical 1980s lists, treating them as one of the smarter post-disco / mutant-funk acts of the era. Fantano's coverage of the band is essentially nonexistent — they are too pre-internet, too obscure, too specific to a particular New York moment that didn't survive into the YouTube era. **The pick is extrapolated; Fantano would likely have preferred Tom Tom Club here, and the only reason Was (Not Was) made the cut is Scaruffi's insistence.** The flag is real, and Tom Tom Club is in the honorable mentions for exactly that reason. But the record is genuinely good — "Knocked Down, Made Small (Treated Like a Rubber Ball)" and "(Return to the Valley of) Out Come the Freaks" are among the best mutant-funk tracks of the early 1980s — and the Ze axis deserves representation on a post-disco list.

##### 10. Shannon — *Let the Music Play* (1984)

The freestyle slot, and the most contested entry on the list. Freestyle is sometimes treated as a separate genre rather than a subgenre of post-disco, but the case for including it here is that freestyle emerged directly from the same Latin-and-Black New York dance-music scene that produced post-disco, used the same drum machines (LinnDrum, the Roland TR-808), and was distributed through the same club-and-radio circuit. Shannon's "Let the Music Play," produced by Mark Liggett and Chris Barbosa, was the breakout single — a #8 pop hit in 1984 that established the freestyle template (broken-beat 808 patterns, melodic synth lines, Latin-inflected vocal phrasing).

The album of the same name is less consistent than the single, which is the standard freestyle problem — the singles were the point, and the albums were assembled around them. But *Let the Music Play* (the album) is a genuine post-disco artifact, with "Give Me Tonight" and the title track standing as two of the best dance singles of 1984. Scaruffi's case was that freestyle deserves a single representative, and Shannon is the canonical one. Fantano's likely preference here is unclear — his coverage of 1980s freestyle is essentially zero. **The pick is extrapolated and flagged.** Stacey Q's *Better Than Heaven* and Lisa Lisa & Cult Jam's *Lisa Lisa & Cult Jam with Full Force* are both defensible alternatives; the Shannon record won on historical primacy.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

Two slots went to coin flip, and both flips landed on the more critically-defensible pick rather than the more culturally-dominant one:

**Flip 1: Liquid Liquid vs Loose Joints / "Is It All Over My Face?" (slot 5).** The argument was about whether the no-wave/dance-punk slot should go to Liquid Liquid's self-titled EP-as-album or to the broader Loose Joints / Russell-adjacent twelve-inch lineage. Loose Joints' "Is It All Over My Face?" (Russell with Steve D'Acquisto, released on West End in 1980, remixed by Larry Levan) is one of the foundational singles of the era, but it does not exist as an album, and the list is an album list. Liquid Liquid won by default and by genuine merit — the band's four 99 Records EPs (self-titled and *Successive Reflexes* in 1981, *Optimo* in 1983, *Dig We Must* in 1984) are coherent and important enough to be treated as an album-equivalent.

**Flip 2: *Off the Wall* vs *Thriller* (slot 4).** The argument was which Jackson record represents post-disco better. *Off the Wall* (1979) is the more genre-canonical pick — closer to disco's rhythmic core, less burdened by rock-crossover impulses, more consistent as a record. *Thriller* (1982) is the more culturally-dominant record, but its strongest tracks ("Beat It," "Billie Jean") are pop and post-punk-adjacent rather than post-disco. *Off the Wall* won the flip and the argument.

A third near-flip almost happened between Madonna's debut *Madonna* and *Like a Virgin* (slot 7). Scaruffi's preference was the debut on genre-purity grounds; Fantano's preference was *Like a Virgin* on production-quality and cultural-impact grounds. *Like a Virgin* won on Rodgers' production, which gives the record a stronger sonic identity than the debut's more anonymous post-disco productions. The flag is the same as elsewhere — Fantano's coverage of pre-2007 Madonna is thin, and the pick is partially extrapolated.

#### Honorable Mentions

**Tom Tom Club — *Tom Tom Club* (1981).** The Tina Weymouth and Chris Frantz Talking Heads side project that produced "Genius of Love," one of the most sampled tracks in hip-hop history. Compass Point connection (recorded there). Lost slot 9 to Was (Not Was) on Scaruffi's preference; would have been a Fantano pick.

**Grace Jones — *Warm Leatherette* (1980)** and ***Living My Life* (1982).** The bookend Compass Point trilogy records. *Warm Leatherette* is the entry point, *Living My Life* is the mature closing chapter. Either could have replaced *Nightclubbing* on a different day; *Nightclubbing* won on the strength of "Pull Up to the Bumper" and "I've Seen That Face Before."

**Arthur Russell — *Calling Out of Context* (posthumous 2004 compilation).** Released by Audika Records in 2004, a decade after Russell's 1992 death. Not eligible for the main list as a contemporaneous album, but it is the record through which most listeners under 40 first encountered Russell, and it contains tracks ("That's Us / Wild Combination," "Make 1, 2") that are as essential as anything on *World of Echo*. **First Thought Best Thought* (posthumous 2006) and *Love Is Overtaking Me* (posthumous 2008) are similar cases.**

**Mtume — *Juicy Fruit* (1983).** The title track is one of the great post-disco / early-boogie singles, sampled by Notorious B.I.G. for "Juicy." Cross-listed with boogie; lost the soul/funk-leaning slot to ESG and the freestyle slot to Shannon.

**Yarbrough & Peoples — *The Two of Us* (1980).** Contains "Don't Stop the Music," one of the era's great dance singles. Lost to Mtume in the boogie-crossover argument, then to Shannon in the freestyle argument, then off the list entirely.

**Taana Gardner — *Taana Gardner* (1981).** West End Records, contains "Heartbeat" (remixed by Larry Levan), one of the foundational Garage tracks. Album-as-artifact is less consistent than the singles; lost the West End slot to nobody — there isn't a West End album on the main list, which is one of the list's weaknesses.

**Loleatta Holloway — *Love Sensation* (1980).** Salsoul, not West End, but adjacent. "Love Sensation" is the vocal sample on Black Box's "Ride on Time" (1989) and dozens of other tracks. Lost to the same album-versus-single problem as Taana Gardner.

**Lisa Lisa & Cult Jam with Full Force — *Lisa Lisa & Cult Jam with Full Force* (1985).** The freestyle alternative to Shannon. "I Wonder If I Take You Home" is the canonical freestyle single; the album won the cultural-impact argument but lost the historical-primacy argument to Shannon.

**Stacey Q — *Better Than Heaven* (1986).** Contains "Two of Hearts." More pop, less freestyle-pure than Shannon or Lisa Lisa. Honorable mention rather than main list.

**Pointer Sisters — *Break Out* (1983).** "Jump (for My Love)," "I'm So Excited," "Automatic." A genuine post-disco crossover record, produced by Richard Perry. Lost the mainstream-pop slot to Madonna and Janet on the principle that two pop slots is already the maximum.

**Cristina — *Sleep It Off* (1984).** The Ze Records vocalist's second album, produced by Don Was. The alternative Ze pick to Was (Not Was); lost on consistency.

**Kid Creole & the Coconuts — *Tropical Gangsters* / *Wise Guy* (1982).** The other Ze Records option. August Darnell's tropical-mutant-disco project. Strong record, lost slot 9 on genre-fit grounds (more tropical than post-disco proper).

**The B-52's — *Mesopotamia* EP (1982).** David Byrne produced. Post-disco-adjacent, more new wave than dance, but the rhythm section work pulls it into the conversation. Off the main list on genre-purity grounds.

**Sister Sledge — *We Are Family* (1979).** Chic produced. Last-year-of-disco rather than first-year-of-post-disco; classified as disco proper for the purposes of this list and addressed elsewhere in the volume.

**Diana Ross — *diana* (1980).** Chic produced. "Upside Down," "I'm Coming Out." Sits exactly on the disco / post-disco hinge; arguments either way. Off the main list because the Chic-as-producers slot is implicitly filled by *Like a Virgin*.

**Indeep — *Last Night a DJ Saved My Life* (1983).** The title single is one of the canonical post-disco-into-early-house records. Album is thin. Off the main list on consistency grounds.

**D-Train — *D-Train* (1982).** Foundational boogie record, "You're the One for Me." Cross-listed with boogie; covered more fully there.

The two structural absences from the main list are West End Records (no single album held up as an album rather than a singles collection) and the broader boogie axis (Mtume, Yarbrough & Peoples, D-Train), which is more comprehensively addressed in the boogie/electro-funk file. Both absences are deliberate. The post-disco list emphasizes the downtown art-rock / Compass Point / Russell axis because that is where the genre's most album-coherent work was made — the singles culture of West End and the boogie circuit produced different kinds of canonical objects, and treating them as albums distorts the picture.

---

## Overall Volume Top 10 — Cross-Sub-Genre

*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano (Cross-Sub-Genre)*

### Debate Summary

Twenty-two sub-genre debates and roughly two hundred and twenty album-length entries collapse here into one ten-record list that has to stand for the whole tradition. The debate was, on the high end, easier than the parallel debate in Volume 2. Four records came in without argument from either critic: *What's Going On*, *Songs in the Key of Life*, *There's a Riot Goin' On*, and *Voodoo*. Scaruffi's all-time American-albums list and Fantano's stated-or-extrapolated retrospective canon overlap on those four cleanly, and the only opening question was sequencing inside the top four. The middle of the list — slots five through eight — was where the work happened: how many James Brown records, whether *Otis Blue* or *I Never Loved a Man* anchors the 1960s portion, where *Maggot Brain* sits given that it functions simultaneously as the psychedelic-soul peak and the P-Funk founding text, and how much weight the volume should give the contemporary tail.

The artist cap was set explicitly before the substantive debate started, on the grounds that any honest cross-genre list of this tradition will be dominated by four or five auteurs unless deliberately constrained. **One album per artist.** Stevie Wonder gets one slot (*Songs in the Key of Life*, edging *Innervisions* on completeness rather than peak intensity); Marvin Gaye gets one slot (*What's Going On*, with *Let's Get It On* and *I Want You* falling to honorable mentions); Prince gets one slot (*Sign o' the Times*, the consensus peak across the funk-rock and Minneapolis files); James Brown gets one slot (*Live at the Apollo*, on the founding-document rationale); Aretha Franklin gets one. The cap eliminated the four-Sly, three-Funkadelic, three-Parliament-Funkadelic scenarios the sub-genre files individually support but that would collapse a volume-wide top ten into a partial discography.

Three places the debate genuinely diverged. **The disco question** — Scaruffi was willing to let Donna Summer's *Bad Girls* in on the strength of the production history; Fantano-extrapolated agreed; the slot went to Prince on a coin flip because both critics conceded the disco wing was being asked to carry more weight than its single best album reasonably could against the singer-songwriter-auteur peaks of 1971-1987. **The Curtis Mayfield question** — Scaruffi rates *Superfly* among the most important American records of the 1970s; the slot lost narrowly to *Blonde* on the contemporary-tail balance argument. **The James Brown selection** — Scaruffi pushed for *In the Jungle Groove*; the project rule preferring contemporaneous releases over reissue compilations broke for *Live at the Apollo* on the founding-text rationale and a Scaruffi-conceded coin flip.

### The Top 10

#### 1. Marvin Gaye — *What's Going On* (Tamla/Motown, 1971)

Recorded at Hitsville USA in Detroit, with sessions at the Hitsville West Coast facility in Los Angeles for overdubs, between June 1970 and March 1971, with Marvin Gaye co-producing for the first time over Berry Gordy's documented initial refusal to release the record. The Funk Brothers — James Jamerson on bass (the legendary "What's Going On" bass line cut in a single take, reportedly with Jamerson lying on his back), Earl Van Dyke on keyboards, Eddie "Bongo" Brown on percussion, Robert White on guitar — provide the rhythm backbone. Eli Fountain's alto-saxophone arpeggio that opens the title track was an accidental warmup phrase that Gaye instructed the engineer to keep. David Van DePitte arranged the strings. The suite structure — songs bleeding into one another without gaps, Marvin's multitracked vocals stacked into a self-choir, sax and conga drifting in from offscreen — was unprecedented at Motown and remains the most influential single piece of conceptual-album architecture in Black American popular music.

The case for *What's Going On* at number one is the consensus case. Scaruffi places it at the top of his Motown coverage and treats it as one of the central American records of the 1970s. Fantano has named it among the greatest albums of all time on multiple year-end retrospectives and on his "Top 200 Albums of All Time" stream. The Stax/Detroit, gospel/soul, jazz/pop divisions that the sub-genre files spend so much energy adjudicating dissolve here — *What's Going On* is, by reasonable critical consensus, the record where the entire tradition this volume covers makes its strongest single argument for itself as art. "Mercy Mercy Me (The Ecology)," "Inner City Blues (Make Me Wanna Holler)," "What's Happening Brother," "God Is Love," and the title track function as movements in a single composition about the Vietnam War, urban poverty, ecological devastation, and the spiritual exhaustion of a Black community that had just lost King, Malcolm, the Panthers, and the optimism of the integrationist 1960s. The record is forty-one minutes long. It has no filler. It is the volume's least controversial number one. Neither critic argued against the placement.

#### 2. Stevie Wonder — *Songs in the Key of Life* (Tamla/Motown, 1976)

Recorded between 1974 and 1976, primarily at Crystal Sound in Los Angeles with additional sessions at Record Plant LA, Hit Factory NYC, and Record Plant Sausalito. Released September 28, 1976 as a double LP plus a four-song bonus 7-inch EP — twenty-one tracks across roughly an hour and forty-five minutes. Wonder is credited as producer, principal arranger, and performer of most of the keyboard, drum, and synthesizer parts. The guest list runs to seventy-plus musicians: Herbie Hancock, George Benson, Bobbi Humphrey, Hubert Laws, Nathan Watts on bass, Greg Phillinganes on additional keyboards, the Tower of Power horn section, vocals from Minnie Riperton and Deniece Williams. The synthesizer work is primarily ARP and Moog — Wonder had become one of the instrument's earliest virtuosic interpreters and pushed the vocabulary further here, particularly on "Sir Duke" and "I Wish."

The album holds the second slot because the classic Stevie period — *Music of My Mind* (1972) through *Songs in the Key of Life* — is, by both critics' independent ranking, one of the two or three greatest single-artist runs in popular music. *Songs in the Key of Life* gets the volume-wide slot over *Innervisions* (1973), *Talking Book* (1972), and *Fulfillingness' First Finale* (1974) on completeness — it is the longest, the most stylistically ranging, the most thoroughly produced, and the record where every preceding album-by-album argument finds its synthesis. "Sir Duke" is the Ellington tribute. "I Wish" is the autobiographical funk anthem. "Isn't She Lovely" is the lullaby for Wonder's daughter Aisha. "As" is the love song stretched across seven minutes of building gospel-soul climax. "Pastime Paradise" is the chord-vocabulary template Coolio would sample for "Gangsta's Paradise." "Village Ghetto Land" is the string-quartet protest piece. Scaruffi rates the run as the high point of his entire R&B canon. Fantano has called it "untouchable" on multiple retrospective streams and has ranked *Songs in the Key of Life* in the top ten of his personal all-time list. The artist cap permits one Stevie album; this is the one.

#### 3. Sly & The Family Stone — *There's a Riot Goin' On* (Epic, 1971)

Recorded primarily at Sly Stone's Bel Air home studio between 1970 and 1971, with additional sessions at Record Plant Sausalito and the Plant Studios. Released November 1, 1971 on Epic, Stone produces and performs most of the instrumental parts himself, overdubbing onto tape that had already been overdubbed onto until the magnetic emulsion thinned. The Family Stone members — Larry Graham on bass, Cynthia Robinson on trumpet, Jerry Martini on saxophone, Greg Errico and Gerry Gibson on drums, Rose Stone on keyboards — appear in various combinations, but the record is fundamentally a solo project under the band's name. Bobby Womack contributes guitar; Billy Preston is rumored on keyboards. The Maestro Rhythm King MRK-2 drum machine, used on "Family Affair" (the first #1 hit recorded almost entirely on a drum machine), defines the album's rhythmic foundation in a way no prior major-label R&B record had attempted.

The case for *Riot* at three is the Scaruffi case. He places it among the greatest American albums ever made, full stop, on a level with *Trout Mask Replica* and *White Light/White Heat*. The structural decisions — the silent title track (a joke and an indictment), the nine-minute "Africa Talks to You ('The Asphalt Jungle')," the quarter-speed reconfiguration of "Thank You" as "Thank You for Talkin' to Me, Africa," the deliberate degradation of the tape itself as an aesthetic principle — make it the volume's most formally radical record. Fantano has explicitly named *Riot* as the Sly Stone record that matters most on multiple retrospectives and rates the album among his all-time top tier. The placement at three reflects the volume's broader balance — *Riot* is more formally extreme than *What's Going On* or *Songs in the Key of Life*, but its position outside both critics' broader cultural canon (Marvin and Stevie are the more frequently cited touchstones) and its singularity as a project keep it from displacing the auteur peaks at one and two. The record where soul music admits the 1960s did not work. The album every record below this one is in some sense reacting to.

#### 4. D'Angelo — *Voodoo* (Virgin, 2000)

Recorded primarily at Electric Lady Studios in New York between 1998 and 1999, released January 25, 2000 on Virgin, produced by D'Angelo with Russell Elevado engineering. The Soulquarians collective is the band: Questlove on drums, James Poyser on keyboards, Roy Hargrove on horns, Pino Palladino on bass (replacing the departed Charlie Hunter for most of the rhythm tracks), with contributions from Q-Tip, Method Man, Redman, Lauryn Hill, Common, and Raphael Saadiq. The recording philosophy was Questlove's "loose-pocket" drumming concept: instruments deliberately slightly out of phase with each other, the click track abandoned, the groove constructed as a human-error system rather than a quantized grid. The Marvin Gaye, Sly Stone, and Prince influences are explicit — Questlove has discussed the band studying *I Want You*, *Riot*, and *Sign o' the Times* as production references — and the resulting album is the most influential R&B record of its quarter-century.

Both critics treat *Voodoo* as the central neo-soul artifact and as one of the defining Black American records of the 2000s. Scaruffi treats it as one of the most important records of its decade across all genres. Fantano has reviewed it repeatedly, placed it in his "best of the 2000s" and "best of the 21st century" lists, and named it among his all-time favorites. "Untitled (How Does It Feel)" is the Prince-influenced ballad that became the commercial moment via its single-take video. "Devil's Pie," produced with DJ Premier, is the hip-hop crossover. "The Root" is the seven-minute slow-burn that opens the second half. "Spanish Joint" is the Latin-jazz piece with Hargrove's horn arrangement. "Africa" is the closing meditation. The album holds the fourth slot because the contemporary tail of the volume needs a representative at the top, both critics agree on the album-as-album, and it functions structurally — production philosophy, instrumental ensemble, song-cycle architecture — as the most direct twenty-first-century descendant of the records at positions one through three.

#### 5. Otis Redding — *Otis Blue: Otis Redding Sings Soul* (Volt/Atco, 1965)

Recorded across two sessions on July 9-10, 1965 at the Stax studio on East McLemore in Memphis — roughly twenty-two studio hours spanning about twenty-eight hours with a Saturday-night break, the compressed timeline that fed the "single twenty-four-hour session" lore. Released September 1965 on Volt with Atco distribution. The Memphis sound at full shape: Booker T. Jones on Hammond organ and piano, Steve Cropper on guitar, Donald "Duck" Dunn on bass, Al Jackson Jr. on drums, with Andrew Love and Wayne Jackson anchoring the Mar-Keys horn section. Redding's voice is the central instrument — a gospel-trained baritone that drags and rushes the beat with a vocabulary of grunts, hums, and held vowels no transcription captures.

The case for *Otis Blue* at five is the volume's pre-1971 anchor case. The record is the consensus southern-soul peak (it tops the Stax file unanswerably), but it is also the album-length argument that the Memphis sound at its peak is a complete artistic statement on the level of the auteur albums that follow. Redding's covers — "Satisfaction" read as want rather than protest, the Sam Cooke tribute "A Change Is Gonna Come" handled like an attempt to push the predecessor back into a room — and his originals — "Respect" (later overtaken by Aretha's reading), "I've Been Loving You Too Long," "Ole Man Trouble" — establish the Stax songwriting house style at its sharpest. Scaruffi treats the album as Redding's masterpiece and as one of the high points of 1960s American music. Fantano (extrapolated, given thin direct coverage of pre-1990 R&B) responds to the band-as-rhythm-machine integrity and the room sound. The album is short, lean, has no filler, and operates as the volume's strongest argument for the 1965-1968 Stax/Memphis axis. *I Never Loved a Man* is the alternate slot-five candidate; the coin came up *Otis Blue* on the founding-document argument.

#### 6. James Brown — *Live at the Apollo* (King, 1963)

Recorded October 24, 1962 at the Apollo Theater in Harlem, released May 1963 on King Records. Brown self-funded the recording over King label head Syd Nathan's objections — Nathan thought a live album of already-released singles was commercial suicide — and is leading the Famous Flames-era band: Bobby Byrd in the vocal trio, Lewis Hamlin's horn section, Les Buie on guitar, Hubert Perry on bass, drummer credits divided across the run. The performance runs barely over half an hour and is built around the "Lost Someone" ballad complex and the closing medley structure that gave a generation of subsequent live albums their template. Brown's exhortation work — the "Are you with me? Are you with me?" rhetorical mode — and the bandleader-as-conductor cuing system document pre-funk soul tipping over the edge.

The case for *Live at the Apollo* at six is the founding-text case. James Brown's place in this volume is unassailable — he is the rhythmic revolutionary who reorganized Black popular music around the One in 1965 with "Out of Sight" and "Papa's Got a Brand New Bag," and the funk vocabulary that runs from his 1969-70 instrumental sides through every subsequent dance tradition is fundamentally his invention. Scaruffi pushed for *In the Jungle Groove* (the 1986 Polydor compilation of 1969-70 sessions); the project rule preferring contemporaneous releases broke for *Live at the Apollo* on a coin flip, with Scaruffi conceding that the live document captures the breath before the revolution in a way no studio compilation can. Fantano (extrapolated, given thin direct coverage of pre-1970 R&B) treats *Live at the Apollo* as canonical. No honest volume-wide list of this tradition can leave James Brown out, and *Live at the Apollo* is the version of the JB argument the broadest possible audience can hear as the founding text of everything that followed.

#### 7. Funkadelic — *Maggot Brain* (Westbound, 1971)

Recorded at United Sound Systems in Detroit between 1970 and 1971, released July 12, 1971 on Westbound. George Clinton produces; the band is Funkadelic's classic early-1970s lineup — Eddie Hazel on lead guitar, Bernie Worrell on keyboards, Billy "Bass" Nelson, Tawl Ross on rhythm guitar, Tiki Fulwood on drums, with Ray Davis, Fuzzy Haskins, Calvin Simon, and Grady Thomas on vocals. The title track is ten minutes and twenty seconds of unaccompanied Eddie Hazel lead guitar over a drained looping rhythm bed, recorded after Clinton's apocryphal instruction to play as if Hazel had just been told his mother had died and then as if he had just learned she was alive again. The other instruments are mixed almost out of audibility.

The case for *Maggot Brain* at seven is the cross-tradition case. The album anchors two sub-genre files — Psychedelic Soul (#2) and P-Funk (#1) — and functions as the bridge between the late-1960s psychedelic experiments at Motown and Stax and the long-form Parliament-Funkadelic project that defined Black rock for the remainder of the decade. Scaruffi treats it as canonical, one of his very few unreserved soul/funk recommendations, and has placed it in his all-time American-rock canon. Fantano has named it explicitly in best-of-the-70s tangents on multiple streams. The case beyond the title track is the surrounding material — "Can You Get to That" as a gospel singalong reverse-engineered from a wake, "Hit It and Quit It" as a Worrell organ workout, "Super Stupid" as a hard-rock blues anticipating Bad Brains, "Wars of Armageddon" as seven minutes of percussion and political invective closing the record. The volume could have placed *Mothership Connection*, *One Nation Under a Groove*, or *Funkentelechy vs. the Placebo Syndrome* here instead, but *Maggot Brain* is the record both critics treat as the tradition's central act of guitar imagination.

#### 8. Aretha Franklin — *I Never Loved a Man the Way I Love You* (Atlantic, 1967)

Recorded across two sessions in early 1967 — the first at Fame Studios in Muscle Shoals on January 24 with Rick Hall engineering and the Fame rhythm section (Tommy Cogbill on bass, Roger Hawkins on drums, Spooner Oldham on Wurlitzer, Jimmy Johnson on guitar, with the Memphis Horns); the second in New York at Atlantic Studios with most of the Muscle Shoals rhythm section flown up after the documented Ted White altercation that ended the Alabama session. Jerry Wexler produced; Tom Dowd engineered the New York work. The album is the moment Aretha stopped being a Columbia jazz-pop singer trying on styles and became Aretha. Wexler's load-bearing decision was to put her at the piano. The records before this had been arranged around her voice; here the piano is the spine and the voice does what gospel singers do alone in a sanctuary.

The case for the eighth slot is the vocal-tradition case. Aretha is the central female vocalist in this volume, and *I Never Loved a Man* is where the entire R&B vocal vocabulary that Whitney Houston, Mariah Carey, Adele, and Beyoncé would inherit is presented in its first fully-realized form. "Respect" inverts the Otis Redding original by making the demand structural rather than situational; the "sock it to me" interjections from Carolyn and Erma Franklin and Cissy Houston turn a song about a couple into a song about a country. "Do Right Woman, Do Right Man" inherits the Sam Cooke vocal tradition. "Dr. Feelgood" defines the gospel-blues hybrid mode. Scaruffi places the album in his upper tier of 1960s records without reservation; Fantano (extrapolated) treats Aretha as a foundational influence on his framework for vocal performance. *Lady Soul* (1968) is the alternate candidate — more consistent, more polished — but *I Never Loved a Man* is the first statement, and the volume needs the first statement here.

#### 9. Frank Ocean — *Blonde* (Boys Don't Cry, 2016)

Self-released August 20, 2016 on Ocean's Boys Don't Cry imprint following the *Endless* visual album the day prior, recorded between 2013 and 2016 across Abbey Road, Henson, and Electric Lady, with credited contributions from Pharrell Williams, James Blake, Buddy Ross, Om'Mas Keith, Tyler the Creator, Beyoncé, André 3000, Kendrick Lamar, Jonny Greenwood, and Kim Burrell. Ocean is the principal writer and producer. The pitch-shifted vocals, ambiguous tempo manipulations, near-absence of percussion across long sections, and structural refusal to deliver conventional R&B song forms make it the most formally radical contemporary R&B record of its decade and the central PBR&B artifact alongside *Channel Orange* (2012).

The case for the ninth slot is the contemporary-tail case. The volume covers 1948 through the present; the post-2010 portion needs representation beyond *Voodoo*; *Blonde* is the consensus contemporary peak. Scaruffi's review is short but positive and places the record at the top of his contemporary R&B page. Fantano has reviewed both *Channel Orange* and *Blonde* and rates them high, and *Blonde* has appeared in multiple best-of-decade and best-of-21st-century retrospective rankings. "Nights" is the structural showpiece — a two-part song that switches halfway through into a slower, lower-pitched register. "Pink + White" is the Pharrell-produced Beyoncé-featuring meditation. "Self Control" is the falsetto ballad. "Solo (Reprise)" is the André 3000 verse that landed as one of the rap features of the decade. The album works as a continuous fifty-six-minute statement, and contemporary R&B as it currently exists is fundamentally in conversation with its formal decisions. Curtis Mayfield's *Superfly* and Lauryn Hill's *Miseducation* were both candidates for the slot; the contemporary balance argument carried.

#### 10. Prince — *Sign o' the Times* (Paisley Park/Warner Bros., 1987)

Released March 31, 1987 on Paisley Park/Warner Bros., the album consolidates three abandoned projects — the *Dream Factory* Revolution album, the *Camille* solo project (Prince's pitch-shifted female-vocal alter ego), and the unreleased *Crystal Ball* triple album — into a sixteen-track, eighty-minute double LP. Prince is credited as writer, producer, principal performer, and engineer; Susan Rogers engineered most of the sessions; the Revolution had been dissolved by the time of release but contributes to some of the material (Wendy Melvoin, Lisa Coleman, and Sheila E. on percussion). The album is conventionally cited as Prince's masterpiece and the strongest album-length statement of his 1980s peak.

The case for the tenth slot is the Minneapolis/funk-rock case. The volume contains two Prince-dominated sub-genre files (Minneapolis Sound and Funk-Rock), both of which place *Sign o' the Times* at #1. Scaruffi is consistently positive on Prince's mid-1980s run; Fantano has placed Prince in his all-time top tier and has named *Sign o' the Times* as his personal favorite Prince album on multiple occasions. The title track updates the *What's Going On* mode for the AIDS-and-crack-and-Reagan moment. "Adore" is the gospel-soul ballad closer. "If I Was Your Girlfriend" is the *Camille*-project gender-perspective experiment. "Housequake" is the James Brown tribute. "U Got the Look" is the rock-crossover single. "The Cross" is the gospel testimony. The album holds the tenth slot through the artist-cap constraint — Prince has multiple records the sub-genre files individually argue for, but *Sign o' the Times* is the consensus single statement. The slot was contested by *Bad Girls* (disco) and *Superfly* (Mayfield); the coin came up Prince on the auteur-peak and dual-sub-genre-anchor arguments.

### Coin-Flip Resolutions

Three irreconcilable positions required formal coin flips.

**Slot five: *Otis Blue* vs *I Never Loved a Man*.** Scaruffi was prepared to argue either record into the top five; Fantano-extrapolated had no strong preference. The substantive argument turned on whether the Stax band-as-rhythm-machine document (*Otis Blue*) or the Aretha-as-arrived-vocalist document (*I Never Loved a Man*) better anchored the volume's 1960s portion. Both critics agreed both records needed to be on the list. The coin came up *Otis Blue* at five and pushed *I Never Loved a Man* to eight. The alternate ranking — Aretha at five, Otis at eight — is defensible on female-vocal-tradition grounds and would not have been wrong.

**Slot six: *Live at the Apollo* vs *In the Jungle Groove*.** Scaruffi's preferred JB record is the 1986 Polydor compilation *In the Jungle Groove*, which gathers the 1969-70 instrumental sessions that constitute his "what is funk exactly" answer. The project's stated preference for contemporaneous releases over reissue compilations broke the question against him; the coin then ran *Live at the Apollo* into the slot on the founding-document argument. Scaruffi conceded the coin without protest. If the project rule had not applied, *In the Jungle Groove* would have taken the slot and *Live at the Apollo* would have moved to honorable mentions, with the volume's pre-1965 anchor responsibilities devolving onto *Otis Blue* alone.

**Slot ten: *Sign o' the Times* vs *Bad Girls* vs *Superfly*.** The three-way coin flip was the volume's most genuinely irreconcilable position. Scaruffi argued for *Superfly* (Curtis Mayfield's most psychedelic statement and the volume's strongest political-soul peak after *What's Going On*). Fantano-extrapolated argued for *Bad Girls* (Donna Summer's commercial-and-artistic peak and the volume's strongest disco statement). The compromise position — Prince's *Sign o' the Times* — split the difference: it is auteur-peak material in the Marvin/Stevie tradition, it is funk-rock in a way that connects to the Funkadelic and Sly portions of the list, and it carries the post-1980 portion of the volume in a way that no other available record on the candidate list can. The coin came up Prince. *Superfly* and *Bad Girls* go to honorable mentions; their absence is the volume's most defensible omission.

### Honorable Mentions

**Curtis Mayfield — *Superfly* (Curtom, 1972).** The political-psychedelic peak the volume could not quite fit. Scaruffi names it alongside *Riot* as the central political-soul artifact of the era; Fantano has retrospectively praised it in best-of-decade contexts. Coin-flipped out of slot ten.

**Donna Summer — *Bad Girls* (Casablanca, 1979).** The disco peak the volume could not quite fit. The Moroder/Bellotte/Summer axis at full expressive range. Coin-flipped out of slot ten. The volume's strongest argument for disco as art-form-equal rather than commercial-form-secondary.

**Stevie Wonder — *Innervisions* (Tamla/Motown, 1973).** The artist cap eliminated the second Stevie slot. *Innervisions* is the leaner, more focused alternate-#2 candidate — "Living for the City," "Higher Ground," "Don't You Worry 'Bout a Thing," and "Golden Lady" are the strongest single-album case in the classic-period run. Lost to *Songs in the Key of Life* on completeness.

**Marvin Gaye — *Let's Get It On* (Tamla/Motown, 1973) and *I Want You* (Tamla/Motown, 1976).** Both records hold #5 and #7 in the Motown file and #2 in the Quiet Storm file (the latter), and either would belong in a top fifteen. Artist cap eliminated.

**Lauryn Hill — *The Miseducation of Lauryn Hill* (Ruffhouse/Columbia, 1998).** The album that the Neo-Soul and Contemporary R&B files both rank at #3 and #4 respectively. The case for a top-ten slot is genuine; lost to *Voodoo* on the neo-soul anchor argument and to *Blonde* on the contemporary-tail argument.

**Aretha Franklin — *Lady Soul* (Atlantic, 1968).** The more consistent Aretha album by some accounts. Lost to *I Never Loved a Man* on the first-statement rationale.

**Herbie Hancock — *Head Hunters* (Columbia, 1973).** The jazz-funk peak. The volume's most direct hinge to Volume 2. The volume could have placed it at slot ten or eleven; the funk-and-soul-as-vocal-tradition argument took precedence. Cross-listed in Volume 2.

**Sly & The Family Stone — *Stand!* (Epic, 1969).** The integrationist Sly the *Riot* response was responding to. Artist cap eliminated. The volume's strongest argument for collective-mode psychedelic soul.

**Parliament — *Mothership Connection* (Casablanca, 1975).** The Afrofuturist conceptual peak. Artist-adjacent cap (one Parliament-Funkadelic slot, taken by *Maggot Brain*) eliminated.

**Erykah Badu — *Mama's Gun* (Motown, 2000) or *Baduizm* (Kedar/Universal, 1997).** The other central neo-soul figure; both records sit in the Neo-Soul file's top four. Lost to *Voodoo* on the consensus-peak argument.

### Volume-Wide Notes

The top ten cherry-picks the #1s of seven sub-genre files (Motown, Stax/Southern Soul, Classic Funk, Psychedelic Soul, Minneapolis Sound, Neo-Soul, PBR&B) and pulls #2-or-lower from three others (Funkadelic's *Maggot Brain* from P-Funk's #1 but the Psychedelic Soul file's #2, Aretha's *I Never Loved a Man* at the Stax file's #2, and *What's Going On* moved out of the Psychedelic Soul file's #7 slot to its primary Motown placement). The volume's representation skews heavily to 1965-1976 — six of the ten records date from those eleven years — with one pre-1965 founding-era record (1963), one from the 1980s (1987), one from 2000, and one from 2016. Both critics agreed the weighting is an accurate reflection of where the volume's mass actually sits, even if the contemporary tail is artistically vital and the pre-1965 founding era is structurally indispensable. Twelve sub-genre files go unrepresented: Doo-Wop, Early R&B (though *Otis Blue* arguably bridges Early R&B and Stax), Philadelphia Soul, Quiet Storm, Contemporary R&B (with *Voodoo* doubling as the file's #6), Jazz-Funk, Disco (coin-flipped out), Eurodisco, Hi-NRG, Post-Disco, Boogie/Electro-Funk, Go-Go, and New Jack Swing. The disco/post-disco/Eurodisco/Hi-NRG block is the most consequential absence — nearly forty entries across four files, zero in the top ten — and the post-disco branch will re-enter the project in Volume 7 (Electronic) where its structural significance for house, techno, and contemporary club culture will get the weight it could not carry here. The Doo-Wop and Early R&B absences reflect the volume's reluctance to treat compilations and singles-driven artifacts as competitive with album-form auteur statements; the Go-Go and New Jack Swing absences reflect the regional and production-style limits of those catalogs; the Quiet Storm and Philadelphia Soul absences reflect the same artist-cap pressure that eliminated *Let's Get It On* and *I Want You*. The top ten is a defensible volume-wide statement of where the tradition's center of mass sits, not the only such list — the honorable mentions and coin-flip alternates preserve the closest alternate rankings.

---

## Closing — Toward the Backbeat of Rock

Twenty-two sub-genre files, three parts, roughly two hundred and twenty album-length entries, seventy-five years of recorded music from the late-1940s urban-blues vocal groups and the early doo-wop street-corner sessions through the most recent PBR&B and disco-revival releases. The volume is more even than Volume 2 was — the Scaruffi/Fantano coverage asymmetry runs in both directions across the part structure rather than weighing the entire volume toward one critic — and the closing should be honest about that as well.

**Coin-flip totals.** Across the twenty-two files, the debate process produced approximately fifty-five coin-flip resolutions — averaging two to three per file, with the bulk concentrated in the most contested files. The coin-flip-heavier files are Stax / Southern Soul (three flips, mostly around the deep-cut wars — James Carr, O.V. Wright, Solomon Burke, the Staple Singers — after the Otis-Redding-*Otis Blue* anchor decision was settled on coherence grounds rather than by flip), Disco (three flips, including the Donna Summer ordering between *Once Upon a Time* and *I Remember Yesterday*, the Chic ordering between *Diana* and *We Are Family*, and the Bee Gees question of *Saturday Night Fever* versus *Spirits Having Flown*), Doo-Wop (three flips, on the Frankie Lymon / Spaniels question, the Platters / Five Keys question, and the Dion / Skyliners question — the white-doo-wop and slick-versus-haunting axes drove most of the irreconcilables), Go-Go (three flips, on the Rare Essence / Junkyard Band question, the Chuck Brown live-versus-1984 question, and the E.U. / Backyard Band question — the regional canon being thin actually surfaced more contested calls, not fewer), boogie-electro-funk (three flips, on Cameo sequencing and the Gap Band question), early R&B (three flips, on the Ray Charles live record, the Atlantic R&B compilation, and the Hank Ballard / Ike & Tina proto-funk slot), Eurodisco (three flips, on ABBA placement, the one-hit-wonder slot, and the slot-10 producer-canon question), Hi-NRG (three flips, on *Welcome to the Pleasuredome* inclusion, the Cowley *School Daze* placement, and Divine / Lime at slot 10), jazz-funk (three flips, on *Mister Magic* vs *Body Heat*, the Roy Ayers / Lonnie Liston Smith question, and the Idris Muhammad / Stanley Clarke category-boundary call), neo-soul (three flips, on Common's *Like Water for Chocolate* versus Maxwell's *Urban Hang Suite* at slot 5, the Erykah Badu *New Amerykah Part Two* versus *Part One* ordering, and Bilal's *Airtight's Revenge* versus *1st Born Second*), and Contemporary R&B (three flips, on Usher's *Confessions* vs *My Way*, Aaliyah's self-titled vs *One in a Million*, and Janet's *janet.* vs *The Velvet Rope* — the Mariah *Mimi* / *Daydream* call also went to a flip in the resolutions section). The coin-flip-lighter files include Motown (two flips, on the singles-canon compilation slot between the Supremes and Smokey Robinson, and on the final auteur-versus-pop slot between Marvin's *Here, My Dear* and the Jackson 5's *ABC*), Funk-Rock (two flips, on RHCP's *Blood Sugar Sex Magik* versus Living Colour's *Vivid* at slots 5-6, and on Prince's *Dirty Mind* versus *1999* at slot 4), and PBR&B (two flips, on FKA twigs' *LP1* versus *MAGDALENE* and on The Weeknd's post-Trilogy ordering, where the contemporary direct-coverage overlap between the two critics produces unusually clean agreement).

**Where Scaruffi and Fantano were most honestly aligned.** Four places. (1) D'Angelo's *Voodoo* (2000) as the central neo-soul artifact. Both critics treat the record as the form's defining peak. Scaruffi's review treats it as one of the most important Black-American records of its decade; Fantano has reviewed *Voodoo* and *Black Messiah* repeatedly and his positions on both are stable and high. The Neo-Soul file is the volume's most consensus-aligned major sub-genre. (2) Stevie Wonder's classic period (*Music of My Mind* through *Songs in the Key of Life*, 1972-1976). Both critics rate the run as one of the great single-artist achievements in popular music. Scaruffi's psychedelic-soul ratings put the run at the top of his entire R&B canon; Fantano has called the run "untouchable" in retrospect commentary. The Psychedelic Soul file's top three are essentially the same on both critics' independent lists. (3) Marvin Gaye's *What's Going On* (1971). Same pattern — both critics treat it as a peak, both rate it near the top of their independent Marvin Gaye lists, both treat its anchor decision (psychedelic soul rather than Motown, despite the Motown release) as obvious. The file's anchor decision was the easiest in the volume. (4) Frank Ocean's *Blonde* (2016) as the central PBR&B artifact. Both critics treat *Blonde* as one of the defining records of its decade. Fantano has reviewed both *Channel Orange* and *Blonde* and rated them high; Scaruffi's review of *Blonde* is short but positive and places the record at the top of his contemporary R&B page. The PBR&B file's number one is the volume's most contemporaneously-arrived-at consensus pick.

**Where Scaruffi's voice dominated by default.** The early files in Part One — Doo-Wop, Early R&B, the pre-1970 portion of Motown, the pre-1970 portion of Stax / Southern Soul — are the volume's most Scaruffi-weighted material. Fantano has essentially no on-record coverage of the 1948-1969 period in this volume, and the canonical placements (the Drifters, the Coasters, Sam Cooke, Ray Charles's Atlantic sides, Aretha Franklin's Atlantic catalog, Otis Redding, Wilson Pickett, the early Motown girl-group catalog) are constructed largely from Scaruffi's positions and from extrapolation of his criteria. Scaruffi's broader R&B-and-soul canon is less developed than his jazz canon, but it is more developed than his disco canon and far more developed than Fantano's pre-1990 R&B coverage, and the volume's earliest five files weight to him accordingly. The flagging convention is in heavy use in those files.

**Where Fantano's voice dominated by default.** Inversely, the late files in Part One — Neo-Soul, Contemporary R&B, New Jack Swing, PBR&B — and the contemporary tail of Part Three are the volume's most Fantano-weighted material. Scaruffi's coverage of post-2000 R&B is thinner than his coverage of the canonical 1960s-1970s material, and Fantano's coverage of the same period is the deepest single-critic coverage in any of the three volumes so far. The PBR&B file in particular reads almost as a direct Fantano canon with Scaruffi positions argued in where his coverage permits — the reverse of how the jazz volume read with the Scaruffi/Fantano polarity flipped.

**Where they consistently diverged.** Three patterns repeat across the volume. (1) **The Motown-versus-Stax question.** Scaruffi systematically prefers Stax / Hi / Muscle Shoals to Motown. He treats the Memphis-and-Alabama southern-soul axis as gospel-and-blues-blooded artistic peaks and treats Motown as a pop-craft factory whose canonical records are charming but artistically secondary. Fantano (extrapolated) is more agnostic and treats the two axes as parallel artistic achievements with different aesthetic frameworks rather than one as artistically superior to the other. The Motown file argues for the Detroit axis against Scaruffi's lower ratings; the Stax file argues for the Memphis axis at the high ratings Scaruffi gives it; the editor's voice is more visible in the Motown file than in any other file in the volume. (2) **The disco question.** Scaruffi has a real disco canon but rates the form's commercial peaks lower than its experimental edges (Arthur Russell, the Salsoul long-form material, the Patrick Adams productions, the Loft underground material) and treats the chart-disco wing with reservation. Fantano (extrapolated) is more sympathetic to the chart-disco material — his contemporary disco-revival reviews suggest he reads Daft Punk and Dua Lipa as direct descendants of Chic and Donna Summer and rates the originals accordingly. The Disco file works the disagreement explicitly and the placements there occasionally split the difference between the two critics' independent rankings. (3) **The psychedelic-soul versus contemporary-R&B coverage split.** Scaruffi's strongest in this volume is the psychedelic-soul wing — the Sly Stone catalog, the Norman Whitfield-era Temptations, the late Marvin Gaye, the classic-period Stevie Wonder. Fantano's strongest in this volume is the PBR&B wing — Frank Ocean, Solange, SZA, FKA twigs, Kelela, Blood Orange. The two critics are essentially carrying different ends of the same tradition's continuum, and the volume tries to weight the part structure accordingly rather than treating either end as dominant.

**Where the volume strains.** Two places, partly overlapping with where Volumes 1 and 2 strained. (1) The pre-1965 Fantano extrapolations, less acute than in Volume 2 because the canon is smaller and the contemporary continuation more direct, but still real in the doo-wop and early-R&B files. (2) Go-Go, which is the volume's most regionally-specific sub-genre and the one where the album-as-unit-of-analysis fits worst — the form is live-performance-centric and the studio catalog is thin enough that the placements are unavoidably partial. The flagging convention catches the strain in both cases.

**What's coming.** Volume 4 covers Rock and its Foundations — the white parallel to the Black secular tradition this volume documents, drawing on the same R&B, soul, gospel, and country roots material that Volume 1 and this volume cover and borrowing from it heavily. The relationship between Volume 3 and Volume 4 is closer than the relationship between Volume 2 (jazz) and Volume 3 (soul/R&B/funk) — rock is not a parallel tradition that shares personnel with R&B and soul, it is largely a derivative tradition that emerged out of the same root material with a white musician class doing the deriving. The early rock and roll covered in Volume 4 is structurally early R&B with white singers (Elvis Presley covering Big Mama Thornton and Arthur Crudup; Buddy Holly working a country-and-R&B fusion; Chuck Berry and Little Richard, who are Black and who anchor in both volumes' early-R&B and early-rock files with extensive cross-listing). The British Invasion of the early 1960s is largely a white British engagement with Black American R&B (the Stones covering Muddy Waters and the Drifters, the Beatles covering Motown and the Shirelles and Smokey Robinson, the Animals covering Sam Cooke and John Lee Hooker). Funk-rock as covered in this volume's Part Two and blues-rock as covered in Volume 1 both preview rock's emergence as its own beast in Volume 4. The cross-listings will run dense in both directions. Volume 4 will name the relationship at the front and work the cross-references throughout.

After Volume 4, the project continues: Volume 5 covers Punk and Metal, Volume 6 covers Hip-Hop, Volume 7 covers Electronic (where the post-disco branch of this volume re-enters), Volume 8 covers Reggae and Caribbean, Volume 9 covers Classical, Volume 10 covers Pop, Volume 11 covers Latin and World, and a closing twelfth volume covers Soundtrack / Score and the residual cross-genre material. The structure is provisional past Volume 4. Some of those will be combined or re-divided.

**Why this volume matters.** The closing claim of the volume is that the tradition it covers — Black American secular popular music in its dance-and-emotion mode, running from doo-wop through PBR&B — is the most globally consequential popular-music tradition of the twentieth century. Every chart-pop record made anywhere in the world since 1950 owes it something. The R&B vocal vocabulary is the basis of essentially all subsequent pop singing — Whitney Houston and Mariah Carey trained their melisma on gospel-and-soul vocalists; Adele's vocal phrasing is southern-soul phrasing; K-pop and J-pop and contemporary Latin pop all run their vocal arrangements through R&B harmony; Auto-Tune as a contemporary vocal effect lives on the chord vocabulary that PBR&B brought into the chart mainstream. The funk rhythm-section vocabulary is the basis of essentially all subsequent dance music — disco, house, techno, drum-and-bass, dubstep, footwork, Afrobeats, reggaeton, all of contemporary mainstream pop's rhythm programming reads back to James Brown's "on the one" reorganization through the descendants this volume traces. The disco 12-inch-DJ-extended-mix format is the basis of all subsequent club culture, all electronic-dance-music infrastructure, all DJ-as-artist conventions. Hip-hop, which the project addresses in Volume 6, is the most direct synthetic descendant of all three of this volume's traditions — it inherits the R&B vocal, the funk break, and the disco DJ format and recombines them into the dominant Black popular music of the post-1979 era and (since roughly 2017) the dominant global popular music period.

Rock has the larger Anglophone canonical apparatus and the deeper critical archive. Classical has the longer recorded history and the more settled canonical infrastructure. Jazz has the more internally rigorous sub-tradition structure. But the music covered in this volume is the one whose vocabulary the rest of the popular-music world is most thoroughly speaking, and a canon project that takes Western popular music seriously has to take the soul, R&B, funk, and disco lineage at least as seriously as it takes the rock canon. Twenty-two sub-genres, two hundred and twenty album-length entries, three phases of a single project — the volume documents the tradition that the rest of the popular music covered in this project is in conversation with whether it knows it or not.

Volume 4, next. The white tradition that grew out of and alongside this one. Read forward.
